Sects and Disagreements

The Wisdom of Ali's Arbitration
Defending Ali's arbitration as wise and following precedents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ali’s acceptance of arbitration was an act of wisdom and mercy, consistent with Quranic and Prophetic precedents.

Core Idea
Ali’s arbitration was the utmost wisdom and mercy for the ummah.

Having established that peace is preferred, the author now applies this principle to Ali’s situation at Siffin. He shows that Ali observed war-weariness and hoped for reconciliation, and that his decision follows the Quranic precedent of appointing arbiters and the Prophet’s example at Hudaybiyya.

Guided Path
1
Read about Ali's observation
Notice that Ali saw signs of weariness in both parties. This is the condition that makes peace-seeking wise, as per Lesson 1.
2
Identify the parallels
The author draws two parallels: Quranic arbitration between spouses and the Prophet's arbitration with the Quraysh at Hudaybiyya. See how these support Ali's action.
3
Summarize the argument
Ali’s arbitration was not a mistake but an act of compassion and hope for reconciliation, following divine and prophetic examples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أَلَّفَ بَيْنَ قُلُوبِهِمْ وَلَكِنَّ اللَّهَ أَلَّفَ بَيْنَهُمْ وَقَالَ فَأَصْبَحْتُمْ بِنِعْمَتِهِ إِخْوَانًا الآيَةَ أَلَّفَ بَيْنَهُمْ فَإِذَا كَانَ التَّأْلِيفُ مَوْجُودًا بِدُونِ الْحَرْبِ وَإِرَاقَةِ الدِّمَاءِ وَتَغَلَّبَ عَلَى ذَلِكَ الطَّمَعُ بِمَا يَعْرِفُهُ مِنْ سَآمَةِ الْفَرِيقَيْنِ وَمَا يَظْهَرُ مِنْ آثَارِ طَلَبِ الرَّاحَةِ مِنْهُمْ وَالظَّفَرِ بِالْأَمْنِ كَانَتِ الْحِكْمَةُ فِي الِاشْتِغَالِ بِهِ لِتَنْدَفِعَ مَعَرَّةُ الْقِتَالِ وَتَتَأَلَّفَ الْقُلُوبُ وَتَتَّحِدَ الْكَلِمَةُ فَعَلِيٌّ لَمَّا عَايَنَ آثَارَ السَّامَةِ فِي الْقَوْمِ رَجَا أَنْ يَحْصُلَ تَأَلُّفُ الْقُلُوبِ بِالتَّحْكِيمِ وَاشْتَغَلَ بِهِ وَهُوَ الْغَايَةُ مِنَ الْحِكْمَةِ وَالنِّهَايَةُ فِي الشَّفَقَةِ وَالْمَرْحَمَةِ عَلَى الْأُمَّةِ عَلَى رَجَاءٍ مِنْهُ أَنْ يُصِيبَ مَنْ أُسْنِدَتْ إِلَيْهِ الْحُكُومَةُ الْحَقَّ وَيَظْفَرَ بِالثَّوَابِ فَيَكُونَ فِي ذَلِكَ الْبُلُوغُ إِلَى مَا جُعِلَتْ لَهُ الْحُرُوبُ عَلَى الْمُسَالَمَةِ وَالْمُصَالَحَةِ اقْتِدَاءً بِمَا أَمَرَ اللَّهُ تَعَالَى مِنْ نَصْبِ الْحَكَمَيْنِ فِي الِاخْتِلَافِ بَيْنَ الزَّوْجَيْنِ وَمَا نَصَبَ رَسُولُ اللَّهِ صَلَّى اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ الْحَكَمَيْنِ فِي اخْتِلَافٍ وَقَعَ بَيْنَهُ وَبَيْنَ أَهْلِ مَكَّةَ وَمَا جَرَى مِنَ الِاصْطِلَاحِ فَالْأُمَّةُ الَّتِي جَاءَتْ فِيهِمُ الشَّهَادَةُ عَنِ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى بِالْآخِرَةِ وَأَمْرُ الدِّينِ الَّذِي عَظُمَ أَمْرُهُ التَّدَبُّرُ وَالنَّظَرُ وَالْإِمَامَةُ الَّتِي الْمَقْصُودُ مِنْهَا التَّأْلِيفُ وَالْأَمْنُ أَحَقُّ أَنْ يُعْمَلَ لَهَا ذَلِكَ ثُمَّ هَؤُلَاءِ لَمَّا أَقَرُّوا بِالْإِمَامَةِ وَالْخِلَافَةِ وَعَلِمُوا بِالْأَسْبَابِ الَّتِي ظَهَرَتْ لَهُمْ مِنْ نَفْسِهِ مِمَّا صَلَحَ بِهَا لِلْخِلَافَةِ ثُمَّ لَمْ تَكُنْ تَغَيَّرَتْ تِلْكَ فِي نَفْسِهِ وَلَا تَبَدَّلَتْ ثُمَّ كَانَ التَّحْكِيمُ مِنْهُ حُكْمًا حَكَمَ بِهِ بِسَبَبِ الْإِمَامَةِ فَيَلْزَمُهُمْ بِحَقِّ إِمَامَتِهِ الْقَبُولُ مِنْهُ وَإِنْ ضَاقَ عَلَيْهِمْ وَجْهُ مَعْرِفَةِ عُذْرِهِ فِي ذَلِكَ ثُمَّ الْعَجَبُ مِنْ غَبَاوَةِ الْخَوَارِجِ أَنَّهُمْ خَطَّأُوهُ فِي التَّحْكِيمِ وَلَوْ أَنَّهُمْ جَعَلُوا ذَلِكَ حُجَّةً لَهُمْ عَلَيْهِ فِيمَا كَانَ مِنْ قَبْلُ إِذْ كَيْفَ لَمْ يَبْدَأْ بِالْمُصَالَحَةِ ثُمَّ كَانَ يَرْجِعُ إِلَى الْحُرُوبِ كَانَ أَقْرَبَ مِنْ أَنْ جَعَلُوا الْأَمْرَ الْأَوَّلَ مِنَ الْحُرُوبِ دَلَالَةً عَلَى خَطَئِهِ فِي الثَّانِي وَهَذِهِ جَهَالَةٌ فَاحِشَةٌ وَتَعَلُّقُهُمْ بِقَوْلِهِ تَعَالَى فَقَاتِلُوا الَّتِي تَبْغِي مُسْتَقِيمٌ لِأَنَّهُ ذُكِرَ بَعْدَ قَوْلِهِ فَأَصْلِحُوا بَيْنَهُمَا وَنَحْنُ كَذَا نَقُولُ إِنَّ الِاشْتِغَالَ يَنْبَغِي أَنْ يَكُونَ أَوَّلًا بِالدُّعَاءِ إِلَى الصُّلْحِ ثُمَّ بَعْدَ وُقُوعِ الْيَأْسِ عَنِ الصُّلْحِ يَرْجِعُ إِلَى الْقِتَالِ لِيَحْصُلَ الْمَقْصُودُ بِذَلِكَ وَهُوَ تَأَلُّفُ الْقُلُوبِ وَاجْتِمَاعُ الْكَلِمَةِ وَاللَّهُ الْمُوَفِّقُ.
He brought together their hearts, but Allah brought them together, and He said: 'And you became, by His favor, brothers' the verse. He brought them together. So when unification exists without war and bloodshed, and desire overcomes that due to what he knows of the weariness of the two parties and what appears of the effects of seeking rest from them and attaining security, wisdom lies in engaging in it to repel the evil of fighting and to unite hearts and unify the word. So Ali, when he witnessed the effects of weariness among the people, hoped that unification of hearts would be achieved through arbitration, and he engaged in it, and it is the ultimate goal of wisdom and the utmost in compassion and mercy for the ummah, in hope that the one to whom the judgment was entrusted might attain the truth and gain reward. In that is the attainment of what wars are intended for: peace and reconciliation, following what Allah Almighty commanded in appointing two arbiters in dispute between spouses, and what the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) appointed two arbiters in a dispute that occurred between him and the people of Mecca, and what took place of reconciliation. So the ummah, about whom testimony from Allah Almighty concerning the Hereafter came, and the matter of religion whose importance is deliberation and consideration, and the imamate whose purpose is unification and security, is more deserving that this be done for them. Then these people, when they acknowledged the imamate and caliphate and knew the reasons that appeared to them from his (Ali's) self that made him fit for the caliphate, and then those reasons did not change in him nor alter, and then the arbitration from him was a ruling he made due to the imamate, then it is incumbent upon them by right of his imamate to accept from him, even if the way of knowing his excuse in that was narrow for them. Then the wonder is at the stupidity of the Khawarij that they considered him mistaken in the arbitration. Had they made that an argument against him concerning what happened before—why did he not begin with reconciliation and then resort to war?—that would be closer than their making the first matter of war an indication of his error in the second. This is gross ignorance. And their clinging to His statement: 'Then fight the one that transgresses' is valid, because it was mentioned after His statement: 'Then make peace between them.' And we likewise say: engagement should first be with invitation to peace, then after despair of peace, one returns to fighting to achieve the desired goal thereby, which is unification of hearts and unity of word. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 1182

Commentary
This passage is a sustained defense of Ali's decision to accept arbitration (tahkim) at Siffin, refuting the Khawarij's charge that it was a sinful error. The author begins by citing Quranic verses showing that Allah can bring hearts together without war, establishing that peace is preferable. He then explains that Ali, seeing war-weariness, sought unification through arbitration as an act of wisdom and mercy. He draws parallels to Quranic arbitration between spouses and the Prophet's arbitration at Hudaybiyya. The author then argues that since the Khawarij accepted Ali's imamate, they must accept his judgment even if they don't fully understand his reasoning. He criticizes their inconsistency: they condemn arbitration but earlier accepted Ali's warfare. Finally, he clarifies the correct understanding of the Quranic command to fight the transgressor: it comes after the command to make peace, so priority is to invite to peace, and only when that fails to fight. The entire passage is structured as a systematic refutation of the Khawarij's position.

السامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some Khawarij believed that any form of arbitration in conflict is a sin because it delays fighting the transgressor.
Correction: The author shows that arbitration is a prophetic practice and that Ali's action was in line with Quranic commands and the Prophet's example.

Checkpoint
Which two precedents does the author cite to support Ali's arbitration?
A) The arbitration at Hudaybiyya and the arbitration between two Muslim groups.
B) The Quranic command to appoint arbiters in marital disputes and the Prophet's arbitration at Hudaybiyya.
C) The arbitration of Abu Bakr and Umar.
D) The arbitration of the early caliphs.

Key Takeaway
Ali’s arbitration was not a mistake but a wise and compassionate act rooted in Quranic and Prophetic precedents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a heated disagreement, do you instinctively seek arbitration and peace, or do you insist on being right regardless of the cost? How does Ali's example of prioritizing unification of hearts challenge your default response? What specific step can you take this week to imitate his wisdom in a relationship where you've been stubborn?
?
The passage says Ali's action was 'the utmost in compassion and mercy for the ummah.' Think of a time when you chose a peaceful resolution over a confrontation. Was your motive truly compassion, or was it convenience or fear? How can you cultivate genuine mercy in your future conflicts, even when you feel you are in the right?
?
Ali followed the precedent of the Prophet's arbitration at Hudaybiyya. Reflect on a situation where you knew the sunnah but found it difficult to follow because it seemed weak or compromising. How does knowing that Ali — the lion of Allah — chose this path change your perception of strength? Are you willing to be seen as 'weak' for the sake of unity, as Ali was?

Sects and Disagreements
intermediate
8 min
Obedience to the Imam and Refuting the Khawarij
Refuting Khawarij arguments and establishing the correct sequence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Khawarij were obligated to accept Ali's arbitration and the logical flaws in their objections, including the correct order of peace then fighting.

Core Idea
Accepting the imam's decision is obligatory; the Khawarij's critique is inconsistent and ignorant.

The author now turns to directly refute the Khawarij. He argues that since they acknowledged Ali as the rightful imam, they must accept his judgment even if they don't fully understand it. He then highlights their inconsistency: they condemned arbitration but had earlier accepted Ali's warfare. Finally, he clarifies the Quranic command to fight the transgressor, showing that peace must be sought first.

Guided Path
1
Understand the argument from imamate
The author says that because the Khawarij accepted Ali's imamate, they are bound by his decisions. This is a key principle of political obedience.
2
Recognize the inconsistency
The author points out that the Khawarij criticized Ali for arbitration after war, but if they had criticized him for not seeking peace first, that would be more logical. Instead, they made the earlier war a proof of error in the later peace.
3
Correct interpretation of the Quranic verse
The verse 'then fight the one that transgresses' (Quran 49:9) comes after 'then make peace between them', so the order is peace first, then fighting only if peace fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أَلَّفَ بَيْنَ قُلُوبِهِمْ وَلَكِنَّ اللَّهَ أَلَّفَ بَيْنَهُمْ وَقَالَ فَأَصْبَحْتُمْ بِنِعْمَتِهِ إِخْوَانًا الآيَةَ أَلَّفَ بَيْنَهُمْ فَإِذَا كَانَ التَّأْلِيفُ مَوْجُودًا بِدُونِ الْحَرْبِ وَإِرَاقَةِ الدِّمَاءِ وَتَغَلَّبَ عَلَى ذَلِكَ الطَّمَعُ بِمَا يَعْرِفُهُ مِنْ سَآمَةِ الْفَرِيقَيْنِ وَمَا يَظْهَرُ مِنْ آثَارِ طَلَبِ الرَّاحَةِ مِنْهُمْ وَالظَّفَرِ بِالْأَمْنِ كَانَتِ الْحِكْمَةُ فِي الِاشْتِغَالِ بِهِ لِتَنْدَفِعَ مَعَرَّةُ الْقِتَالِ وَتَتَأَلَّفَ الْقُلُوبُ وَتَتَّحِدَ الْكَلِمَةُ فَعَلِيٌّ لَمَّا عَايَنَ آثَارَ السَّامَةِ فِي الْقَوْمِ رَجَا أَنْ يَحْصُلَ تَأَلُّفُ الْقُلُوبِ بِالتَّحْكِيمِ وَاشْتَغَلَ بِهِ وَهُوَ الْغَايَةُ مِنَ الْحِكْمَةِ وَالنِّهَايَةُ فِي الشَّفَقَةِ وَالْمَرْحَمَةِ عَلَى الْأُمَّةِ عَلَى رَجَاءٍ مِنْهُ أَنْ يُصِيبَ مَنْ أُسْنِدَتْ إِلَيْهِ الْحُكُومَةُ الْحَقَّ وَيَظْفَرَ بِالثَّوَابِ فَيَكُونَ فِي ذَلِكَ الْبُلُوغُ إِلَى مَا جُعِلَتْ لَهُ الْحُرُوبُ عَلَى الْمُسَالَمَةِ وَالْمُصَالَحَةِ اقْتِدَاءً بِمَا أَمَرَ اللَّهُ تَعَالَى مِنْ نَصْبِ الْحَكَمَيْنِ فِي الِاخْتِلَافِ بَيْنَ الزَّوْجَيْنِ وَمَا نَصَبَ رَسُولُ اللَّهِ صَلَّى اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ الْحَكَمَيْنِ فِي اخْتِلَافٍ وَقَعَ بَيْنَهُ وَبَيْنَ أَهْلِ مَكَّةَ وَمَا جَرَى مِنَ الِاصْطِلَاحِ فَالْأُمَّةُ الَّتِي جَاءَتْ فِيهِمُ الشَّهَادَةُ عَنِ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى بِالْآخِرَةِ وَأَمْرُ الدِّينِ الَّذِي عَظُمَ أَمْرُهُ التَّدَبُّرُ وَالنَّظَرُ وَالْإِمَامَةُ الَّتِي الْمَقْصُودُ مِنْهَا التَّأْلِيفُ وَالْأَمْنُ أَحَقُّ أَنْ يُعْمَلَ لَهَا ذَلِكَ ثُمَّ هَؤُلَاءِ لَمَّا أَقَرُّوا بِالْإِمَامَةِ وَالْخِلَافَةِ وَعَلِمُوا بِالْأَسْبَابِ الَّتِي ظَهَرَتْ لَهُمْ مِنْ نَفْسِهِ مِمَّا صَلَحَ بِهَا لِلْخِلَافَةِ ثُمَّ لَمْ تَكُنْ تَغَيَّرَتْ تِلْكَ فِي نَفْسِهِ وَلَا تَبَدَّلَتْ ثُمَّ كَانَ التَّحْكِيمُ مِنْهُ حُكْمًا حَكَمَ بِهِ بِسَبَبِ الْإِمَامَةِ فَيَلْزَمُهُمْ بِحَقِّ إِمَامَتِهِ الْقَبُولُ مِنْهُ وَإِنْ ضَاقَ عَلَيْهِمْ وَجْهُ مَعْرِفَةِ عُذْرِهِ فِي ذَلِكَ ثُمَّ الْعَجَبُ مِنْ غَبَاوَةِ الْخَوَارِجِ أَنَّهُمْ خَطَّأُوهُ فِي التَّحْكِيمِ وَلَوْ أَنَّهُمْ جَعَلُوا ذَلِكَ حُجَّةً لَهُمْ عَلَيْهِ فِيمَا كَانَ مِنْ قَبْلُ إِذْ كَيْفَ لَمْ يَبْدَأْ بِالْمُصَالَحَةِ ثُمَّ كَانَ يَرْجِعُ إِلَى الْحُرُوبِ كَانَ أَقْرَبَ مِنْ أَنْ جَعَلُوا الْأَمْرَ الْأَوَّلَ مِنَ الْحُرُوبِ دَلَالَةً عَلَى خَطَئِهِ فِي الثَّانِي وَهَذِهِ جَهَالَةٌ فَاحِشَةٌ وَتَعَلُّقُهُمْ بِقَوْلِهِ تَعَالَى فَقَاتِلُوا الَّتِي تَبْغِي مُسْتَقِيمٌ لِأَنَّهُ ذُكِرَ بَعْدَ قَوْلِهِ فَأَصْلِحُوا بَيْنَهُمَا وَنَحْنُ كَذَا نَقُولُ إِنَّ الِاشْتِغَالَ يَنْبَغِي أَنْ يَكُونَ أَوَّلًا بِالدُّعَاءِ إِلَى الصُّلْحِ ثُمَّ بَعْدَ وُقُوعِ الْيَأْسِ عَنِ الصُّلْحِ يَرْجِعُ إِلَى الْقِتَالِ لِيَحْصُلَ الْمَقْصُودُ بِذَلِكَ وَهُوَ تَأَلُّفُ الْقُلُوبِ وَاجْتِمَاعُ الْكَلِمَةِ وَاللَّهُ الْمُوَفِّقُ.
He brought together their hearts, but Allah brought them together, and He said: 'And you became, by His favor, brothers' the verse. He brought them together. So when unification exists without war and bloodshed, and desire overcomes that due to what he knows of the weariness of the two parties and what appears of the effects of seeking rest from them and attaining security, wisdom lies in engaging in it to repel the evil of fighting and to unite hearts and unify the word. So Ali, when he witnessed the effects of weariness among the people, hoped that unification of hearts would be achieved through arbitration, and he engaged in it, and it is the ultimate goal of wisdom and the utmost in compassion and mercy for the ummah, in hope that the one to whom the judgment was entrusted might attain the truth and gain reward. In that is the attainment of what wars are intended for: peace and reconciliation, following what Allah Almighty commanded in appointing two arbiters in dispute between spouses, and what the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) appointed two arbiters in a dispute that occurred between him and the people of Mecca, and what took place of reconciliation. So the ummah, about whom testimony from Allah Almighty concerning the Hereafter came, and the matter of religion whose importance is deliberation and consideration, and the imamate whose purpose is unification and security, is more deserving that this be done for them. Then these people, when they acknowledged the imamate and caliphate and knew the reasons that appeared to them from his (Ali's) self that made him fit for the caliphate, and then those reasons did not change in him nor alter, and then the arbitration from him was a ruling he made due to the imamate, then it is incumbent upon them by right of his imamate to accept from him, even if the way of knowing his excuse in that was narrow for them. Then the wonder is at the stupidity of the Khawarij that they considered him mistaken in the arbitration. Had they made that an argument against him concerning what happened before—why did he not begin with reconciliation and then resort to war?—that would be closer than their making the first matter of war an indication of his error in the second. This is gross ignorance. And their clinging to His statement: 'Then fight the one that transgresses' is valid, because it was mentioned after His statement: 'Then make peace between them.' And we likewise say: engagement should first be with invitation to peace, then after despair of peace, one returns to fighting to achieve the desired goal thereby, which is unification of hearts and unity of word. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 1182

Commentary
This passage is a sustained defense of Ali's decision to accept arbitration (tahkim) at Siffin, refuting the Khawarij's charge that it was a sinful error. The author begins by citing Quranic verses showing that Allah can bring hearts together without war, establishing that peace is preferable. He then explains that Ali, seeing war-weariness, sought unification through arbitration as an act of wisdom and mercy. He draws parallels to Quranic arbitration between spouses and the Prophet's arbitration at Hudaybiyya. The author then argues that since the Khawarij accepted Ali's imamate, they must accept his judgment even if they don't fully understand his reasoning. He criticizes their inconsistency: they condemn arbitration but earlier accepted Ali's warfare. Finally, he clarifies the correct understanding of the Quranic command to fight the transgressor: it comes after the command to make peace, so priority is to invite to peace, and only when that fails to fight. The entire passage is structured as a systematic refutation of the Khawarij's position.

Key Terms
الإمامة
الخوارج
التحكيم
الصّلح
القتال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Khawarij thought that the Quranic command to fight the transgressor (Quran 49:9) implies immediate war without prior peace efforts.
Correction: The author points out that the verse is preceded by a command to make peace, so peace must be attempted first.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main argument against the Khawarij's claim that Ali erred in arbitration?
A) The Khawarij never really accepted Ali's imamate.
B) The Khawarij should have criticized him for not seeking peace first, but instead they criticized him for seeking peace after war.
C) The Khawarij misinterpreted the Quranic verse about fighting.
D) Both B and C.

Key Takeaway
Obedience to the imam requires accepting his judgment, and the Quranic order is peace first, then fighting only as a last resort.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever insisted on a critical opinion about a leader's decision while ignoring your own limited knowledge? This lesson demands you consider: can you accept authority even when the reasoning is obscure? What would it cost you to withhold judgment and submit?
?
When you use a Quranic verse to justify a position, do you first check its full context and the sequence of commands? How often have you, like the Khawarij, used a proof-text without considering what precedes it? What change will you make today in how you approach scripture?
?
The author says the Khawarij's criticism was 'gross ignorance' because they were inconsistent. Where in your life are you inconsistent—criticizing someone's later action while ignoring the earlier ones that contradict your critique? Will you humbly reexamine your own logic today?

The Khawarij's Dilemma: Dialogue vs. Fighting
Nasafi poses a four-branch dilemma refuting the Khawarij's inconsistency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Nasafi uses a dialectical dilemma to prove the Khawarij logically condemn themselves whether they admit error or not.

Core Idea
The Khawarij's own logic traps them in inevitable self-contradiction.

The Khawarij insisted that anyone who accepted arbitration after the Battle of Siffin was a disbeliever, yet they themselves engaged in dialogue with Ali and Ibn Abbas while delaying fighting. Imam al-Nasafi exposes this inconsistency through a simple but powerful dilemma. By following the four possible answers the Khawarij could give, you will see that each one forces them to admit either disbelief or error—the very charge they leveled against others.

Guided Path
1
Read the dilemma
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. The first sentence poses the question: do the Khawarij become disbelievers by debating Ali and Ibn Abbas while postponing fighting?
2
Trace the four possible answers
Identify each branch: (1) They say yes, they become disbelievers. (2) They say no, then ask if they were mistaken or correct. (3) If mistaken, they admit error = disbelief. (4) If correct, then Ali's delay by arbitration is also correct.
The argument forces a concession regardless of their response.
3
Understand the analogy to Ali
Notice how the last branch draws a direct parallel: if the Khawarij delayed fighting hoping for return to truth, then Ali did the same through arbitration. Consistency demands they accept Ali's correctness.
This refutes their core accusation against Ali.
4
Historical note: Ali's own argument
The passage mentions that Ali himself used this exact reasoning, leading many Khawarij to repent. Reflect on the power of this logical approach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مُنَاقَشَةُ الْخَوَارِجِ فِي قَوْلِهِمْ فِي التَّحْكِيمِ
وَيُقَالُ لِلْخَوَارِجِ: إِذَا اشْتَغَلْتُمْ بِمُنَاظَرَةِ ابْنِ عَبَّاسٍ ثُمَّ بَعْدَ ذَلِكَ بِمُنَاظَرَةِ عَلِيٍّ رَضِيَ اللَّهُ عَنْهُمَا وَدَعَوْتُمُوهُمَا إِلَى مَا اعْتَقَدْتُمْ وَتَرَكْتُمْ قِتَالَهُمَا فِي تِلْكَ الْمُدَّةِ رَجَاءَ أَنْ يَعُودَا إِلَى رَأْيِكُمْ أَكْفَرْتُمْ بِذَلِكَ أَمْ لَا؟ فَإِنْ قَالُوا: نَعَمْ فَقَدْ أَقَرُّوا عَلَى أَنْفُسِهِمْ بِالْكُفْرِ، وَإِنْ قَالُوا: لَا. قِيلَ: أَفَكُنْتُمْ فِي ذَلِكَ مُخْطِئِينَ أَمْ مُصِيبِينَ؟ فَإِنْ قَالُوا: كُنَّا مُخْطِئِينَ. فَقَدْ أَقَرُّوا عَلَى أَنْفُسِهِمْ بِالْخَطَأِ وَهُوَ كُفْرٌ عِنْدَهُمْ، وَإِنْ قَالُوا: كُنَّا مُصِيبِينَ. قِيلَ: وَلِمَ كُنْتُمْ كَذَلِكَ وَقَدْ تَرَكْتُمْ قِتَالَهُ مُدَّةَ مُنَاظَرَتِكُمْ إِيَّاهُ وَهُوَ كَافِرٌ فَكَذَلِكَ عَلِيٌّ لَا يَصِيرُ كَافِرًا بِتَرْكِهِ مُقَاتَلَةَ أُولَئِكَ؟ فَإِنْ قَالُوا: إِنَّا تَرَكْنَا قِتَالَنَا رَجَاءَ حُصُولِ الْمَقْصُودِ وَهُوَ رُجُوعُهُ إِلَى الْحَقِّ بِدُونِ الْحَاجَةِ إِلَى الْمُقَاتَلَةِ. قِيلَ: فَكَذَا عَلِيٌّ إِلَّا أَنَّهُ اشْتَغَلَ بِالتَّحْكِيمِ لِهَذَا، وَحُكِيَ أَنَّ عَلِيًّا احْتَجَّ عَلَيْهِمْ بِهَذَا بِعَيْنِهِ، فَكَانَ ذَلِكَ سَبَبَ رُجُوعِ كَثِيرٍ مِنْهُمْ عَنْ ضَلَالَتِهِ. مَا يَحْكُونَ أَنَّ عَلِيًّا شَكَّ فِي نَفْسِهِ حَيْثُ لَمْ يَصِفْهَا بِإِمْرَةِ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ وَأَجَابَهُمْ وَقْتَ التَّحْكِيمِ إِلَى أَنْ لَا يُكْتَبَ عَلَى أَمِيرِ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ. كَلَامٌ فَاسِدٌ، فَإِنَّهُ لَمْ يَشُكَّ فِي ذَلِكَ بَلْ أَرَادَ بِذَلِكَ حَسْمَ الشَّغَبِ، وَقَدْ فَعَلَ رَسُولُ اللَّهِ
Discussion of the Khawarij concerning their statement about the arbitration. It is said to the Khawarij: When you engaged in debating Ibn Abbas and then after that in debating Ali (may Allah be pleased with them) and invited them to what you believed, and you abandoned fighting them during that period hoping that they would return to your opinion, do you thereby become disbelievers or not? If they say: Yes, then they have admitted against themselves disbelief. If they say: No. It is said: Then were you in that mistaken or correct? If they say: We were mistaken. They have admitted against themselves error, which is disbelief according to them. If they say: We were correct. It is said: Why were you so, when you abandoned fighting him during the period of your debating him while he was a disbeliever? So likewise, Ali does not become a disbeliever by abandoning fighting those people. If they say: We abandoned our fighting hoping for the attainment of the goal, which is his return to the truth without the need for fighting. It is said: So too Ali, except that he engaged in arbitration for this reason. And it is narrated that Ali argued against them with this very same point, and that was the cause of many of them returning from their misguidance. What they narrate that Ali doubted in himself when he did not describe himself as Commander of the Faithful and agreed with them at the time of arbitration not to write 'Commander of the Faithful' – this is corrupt speech. For he did not doubt in that, but rather intended by that to cut off discord. And the Messenger of Allah indeed did...
Source pages p. 1183

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical refutation of the Khawarij. It first poses a dilemma: if the Khawarij engaged in dialogue with Ibn Abbas and Ali while suspending fighting, they either become disbelievers or not. If they say yes, they admit their own disbelief. If no, then they must be either correct or mistaken. If mistaken, that is disbelief by their own standard. If correct, then they delayed fighting for hope of return to truth, which is exactly what Ali did when he accepted arbitration for the same reason—so Ali is also correct. The author adds that Ali himself used this argument, leading many Khawarij to repent. Then he addresses the claim that Ali doubted his own title by agreeing to drop 'Commander of the Faithful' from the arbitration document. He dismisses this as corrupt speech, explaining that Ali did not doubt but aimed to cut off discord, and cites an incomplete prophetic precedent (likely the treaty of Hudaybiyya where the Prophet agreed to drop his title).

Key Terms
مُنَاظَرَة
التحكيم
الخوارج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the Khawarij's stance was logically consistent because they only rejected arbitration, not dialogue.
Correction: The dilemma shows that their own practice of dialogue while delaying fighting is indistinguishable from arbitration in its intent, leading to self-condemnation.

Checkpoint
According to the dilemma, if the Khawarij answer that they were correct in debating Ali while delaying fighting, what conclusion follows?
A) They admit disbelief.
B) They admit error.
C) They must accept that Ali's arbitration is also correct.
D) They must resume fighting immediately.

Key Takeaway
The Khawarij's own actions logically force them either to admit disbelief or to accept Ali's arbitration as correct, refuting their core accusation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there areas in your life where you condemn others for something you yourself practice? How does the Khawarij's dilemma expose your own double standards? Write one step you will take today to align your judgments with consistency.
?
The Khawarij thought they were sincere in their fight for truth, but their logic trapped them. What deeply held conviction of yours might be undermined by your own actions? Are you willing to examine it with the same rigorous logic this passage applies?
?
If you were in the place of the Khawarij, would you have the humility to accept that your own standard condemns you when you debated Ibn Abbas? When was the last time you admitted that your own argument turned against you? What would it take for you to repent like those who returned from misguidance?

Ali's Alleged Doubt: The Title of Commander
Nasafi refutes the claim that Ali doubted his imamate by dropping his title.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Nasafi refutes the Khawarij's claim that Ali doubted his own imamate by dropping his title at arbitration.

Core Idea
Ali's removal of his title was a tactical move to end discord, not a sign of doubt.

The Khawarij also argued that Ali's agreement to drop the phrase 'Commander of the Faithful' from the arbitration document proved he doubted his own legitimacy. Imam al-Nasafi dismisses this as corrupt speech, explaining that Ali's intention was to cut off discord, following the precedent of the Prophet (peace be upon him) at Hudaybiyya. This lesson examines that rebuttal and its basis in prophetic practice.

Guided Path
1
Read the Khawarij claim
The Khawarij claimed that Ali doubted himself because he did not insist on the title 'Commander of the Faithful' in the arbitration agreement.
2
Understand the refutation
The author states this is corrupt speech (كَلَامٌ فَاسِدٌ). Ali did not doubt; he aimed to cut off discord (حَسْمَ الشَّغَبِ).
3
Connect to prophetic precedent
The passage begins to cite the Prophet's action (likely the Treaty of Hudaybiyya where he agreed to remove his title). Reflect on how this parallel validates Ali's decision.
The text cuts off, but the reference is clear to those familiar with the seerah.
4
Apply the lesson
Consider how this refutation protects the integrity of Ali's leadership and highlights the Khawarij's superficial reading of events.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

A) He doubted his imamate.

Ali's removal of his title was a wise tactic to end discord, following the Prophet's example, not a sign of doubt.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a situation where you insisted on a personal title, recognition, or status even though it caused friction or division. What would Ali’s example of dropping his title for the sake of unity challenge you to do differently this week? Name one concrete action you can take.
?
Have you ever judged someone’s faith or sincerity based on a single external action, without understanding their true intention? How does the lesson’s warning against 'corrupt speech' and its call to look at the deeper objective (cutting off discord) change your approach to judging others?
?
Ali followed the Prophet’s example by prioritizing peace over personal status. Are you willing to sacrifice your own reputation or pride to bring people together? What is holding you back from taking that step today?

6 min
Refutation of the Khawarij on Arbitration
The author refutes the Khawarij by turning their own argument against them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the Khawarij by exposing an inconsistency in their own actions regarding fighting and arbitration.

Core Idea
The Khawarij's own delay in fighting disproves their objection to Ali's arbitration.

This passage presents a powerful refutation of the Khawarij's stance on arbitration (tahkīm). The author constructs a dilemma based on the Khawarij's own behavior: they delayed fighting Ibn Abbas and Ali while debating them, yet they condemned Ali for delaying fighting through arbitration. By turning their argument against them, the author reveals a self-contradiction that led many Khawarij to abandon their error.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation. Notice the dialectical structure: the author poses a series of questions to the Khawarij.
2
Identify the dilemma
The argument hinges on a dilemma: either the Khawarij committed disbelief by delaying fighting, or they were correct. Trace each horn and its consequences.
The author forces them into a contradiction regardless of their answer.
3
See how the Khawarij's defense is turned against them
The Khawarij claim they delayed fighting hoping for the opponent's return to truth. The author points out that Ali's arbitration had the same intention.
This equalizes their situations, refuting their objection.
4
Reflect on the historical outcome
Note that Ali himself used this argument, causing many Khawarij to repent. This shows the effectiveness of the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال للخوارج: إذا اشتغلتم بمناظرة ابن عباس ثم بعد ذلك بمناظرة علي - رضي الله عنهما - ودعوتهما إلى ما اعتقدتم، وتركتم قتاله في تلك المدة رجاء أن يعود إلى رأيكم أكفرتم بذلك أم لا؟ فإن قالوا: نعم فقد أقروا على أنفسهم بالكفر، وإن قالوا: لا. قيل : أفكنتم في ذلك مخطئين أم مصيبين؟ فإن قالوا: كنا مخطئين. فقد أقروا على أنفسهم بالخطأ وهو كفر عندهم، وإن قالوا: كنا مصيبين. قيل : ولم كنتم كذلك وقد تركتم قتاله مدة مناظرتكم إياه وهو كافر فكذلك علي لا يصير كافرًا بتركه مقاتلة أولئك؟ فإن قالوا : إنا تركنا قتالنا رجاء حصول المقصود وهو رجوعه إلى الحق بدون الحاجة إلى المقاتلة. قيل: فكذا علي إلا أنه اشتغل بالتحكيم لهذا ، وحكي أن عليا احتج عليهم بهذا بعينه، فكان ذلك سبب رجوع كثير منهم عن ضلالته.
And it is said to the Khawarij: When you engaged in debate with Ibn Abbas and then with Ali - may Allah be pleased with them both - and called them to what you believed, and left off fighting them during that period hoping that he would return to your opinion, did you thereby commit disbelief or not? If they say: Yes, then they have admitted to disbelief against themselves. And if they say: No, it is said: Were you in that mistaken or correct? If they say: We were mistaken, then they have admitted to error against themselves, and error according to them is disbelief. And if they say: We were correct, it is said: Then why were you so, while you left off fighting him for the duration of your debate with him, considering him a disbeliever? So likewise, Ali does not become a disbeliever by leaving off fighting those people. If they say: We left off our fighting hoping to achieve the objective, that is, his return to the truth without need for fighting. It is said: So too with Ali, except that he engaged in arbitration for this reason. And it is narrated that Ali argued against them with this very point, and that was the cause of many of them returning from their misguidance.
Source pages p. 1183

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Khawarij concerning their stance on arbitration (tahkīm). The author constructs a dilemma: he asks the Khawarij whether their own delay in fighting Ibn Abbas and Ali—while they debated them in hopes of convincing them—constituted disbelief (kufr) or not. If they say yes, they admit to kufr; if no, they are asked whether they were correct or mistaken in that delay. If they say mistaken, then by their own standard, error is kufr. If they say correct, the author points out the inconsistency: they delayed fighting someone they considered a disbeliever, so why should Ali be considered a disbeliever for delaying to fight them while he pursued arbitration? The Khawarij's likely response is that they delayed hoping for the opponent's return to the truth without fighting. The author then turns this against them: Ali's arbitration was done with the same intention. He concludes by noting that Ali himself used this argument, leading many Khawarij to abandon their error.

Key Terms
الخوارج
التحكيم
الكفر
الخطأ
القتال
الصحابة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Khawarij believed that Ali's arbitration was an act of disbelief, while their own delay in fighting was justified because they were seeking debate.
Correction: The author shows that the Khawarij's own delay in fighting while debating is logically equivalent to Ali's delay through arbitration. Thus, if they condemn Ali, they must condemn themselves, revealing their stance as self-contradictory.

Checkpoint
What does the author use to refute the Khawarij's objection to Ali's arbitration?
A) The Qur'anic verse on fighting transgression
B) Their own delay in fighting while debating
C) The consensus of the Sahaba
D) The example of the Prophet

Key Takeaway
The Khawarij's own actions refute their condemnation of Ali's arbitration, as both sought to avoid bloodshed in hope of returning the opponent to truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever condemned someone for a fault you yourself commit? How does the Khawarij's self-contradiction expose the hypocrisy in your own judgments of others? Will you repent from this and seek forgiveness before you are judged with the same measure?
?
When you hope someone returns to the truth, do you give them the time and space to do so, or do you demand immediate compliance? The Khawarij's impatience led them to declare war on Muslims—where in your life has your impatience with others' growth led you to harshness? Commit today to follow Ali's example: seek reconciliation without compromising truth.
?
If any error in your understanding of Islam were considered disbelief, how would you defend your own fallibility? Does this passage humble you to realize that you too are prone to error and need Allah's mercy? How will you cultivate humility when dealing with differing opinions among Muslims?

Strategic Silence: The Prophet's Example and Ali's Imamate
The author denies Ali's doubt by citing the Prophet's precedent in Hudaybiyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the claim that Ali doubted his imamate by appealing to the Prophet's precedent in the Treaty of Hudaybiyya.

Core Idea
The Prophet's precedent proves strategic silence is not doubt.

This passage defends Ali against a historical accusation by showing that his actions followed the Prophet's own example. The author argues that omitting a title for the sake of peace is not a sign of doubt but of wisdom. By reading this, you'll see how theological arguments often hinge on scriptural precedent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the accusation
Read the passage and note what the Khawarij claimed about Ali: that he doubted his imamate because he omitted the title 'Amir al-Mu'minin' during arbitration.
The accusation is based on an action, not a statement.
2
Analyze the refutation
The author states that Ali's intent was to cut off discord (ḥasm al-shaghb), not due to doubt. This is a defensive tactic.
Note the phrase 'kalam fāsid' – 'corrupt speech' – dismissing the claim.
3
Observe the prophetic analogy
The author draws a parallel with the Prophet ﷺ at Hudaybiyya, who agreed to omit 'Rasul Allah' to secure peace. The Prophet did not doubt his prophethood.
The analogy is the core of the argument; the Prophet is the best example (uswa ḥasana).
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author warns that criticizing Ali on this point implicitly criticizes the Prophet, which amounts to disbelief (kufr). Understand the gravity of the argument.
This shows how defending the Companions is tied to defending the Prophet.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما يحكون أن عليا شك في نفسه حيث لم يصفها بإمرة المؤمنين وأجابهم وقت التحكيم إلى أن لا يكتب على أمير المؤمنين. كلام فاسد، فإنه لم يشك في ذلك بل أراد بذلك حسم الشغب، وقد فعل رسول الله ﷺ مثل ذلك لما كتبت الموادعة بينه وبين سهيل بن عمرو فامتنع سهيل عن ذلك، وقال : لو أقررنا بأنك رسول الله ما قاتلناك، ولم يكن رسول الله ﷺ شاكًا في رسالة نفسه بل فعل قطعًا للشغب، وفي رسول الله ﷺ أسوة حسنة، وهذا الطعن عائد على رسول الله ﷺ وهو كفر، والله الموفق.
What they relate that Ali doubted himself when he did not describe it with 'Commander of the Faithful' and responded to them at the time of arbitration by not writing it as 'Commander of the Faithful' — that is corrupt speech. For he did not doubt that, but rather intended thereby to cut off discord. And the Messenger of Allah ﷺ did the like when the truce was written between him and Suhayl ibn Amr, and Suhayl refused that and said: 'If we acknowledged that you are the Messenger of Allah, we would not have fought you.' And the Messenger of Allah ﷺ was not in doubt about his own message, but rather did it to cut off discord. And in the Messenger of Allah ﷺ there is a good example. And this accusation rebounds upon the Messenger of Allah ﷺ and it is disbelief. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 1183

Commentary
This passage refutes the Khawarij's claim that Ali doubted his own imamate because he agreed to omit the title 'Amir al-Mu'minin' (Commander of the Faithful) during the arbitration. The author states that this allegation is baseless; Ali intended only to cut off discord (ḥasm al-shaghb). He then draws a parallel with the Prophet Muhammad ﷺ during the Treaty of Hudaybiyya, when he agreed to remove the phrase 'Rasul Allah' from the truce document at the insistence of Suhayl ibn Amr. The Prophet did not doubt his prophethood but acted strategically to prevent conflict. The author argues that criticizing Ali on this point implicitly criticizes the Prophet, and such criticism amounts to disbelief (kufr). This argument uses the principle that the Prophet is the best example (uswa ḥasana), so following his precedent is praiseworthy.

Correction: The author shows it was a strategic move to prevent discord, following the Prophet's own example.

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's example shows that strategic omissions for the sake of peace do not indicate doubt, and criticizing Ali on this ground indirectly criticizes the Prophet, which is disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever insisted on a title, credit, or public recognition even when dropping it would have preserved unity? What does the Prophet's example at Hudaybiyya teach you about your own pride today?
?

?
The author declares that criticizing Ali on this point is equivalent to criticizing the Prophet and constitutes disbelief. Does this make you rethink any casual criticism you may have harbored about early Islamic figures? What step will you take to guard your tongue from such judgments?

The Sunni Principle of Refraining from Criticizing the Sahaba
The author states the Ahl al-Sunnah principle of refraining from vilifying the Companions, with justification and example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni principle of refraining from vilifying the Companions, its justification, and how it is applied to specific issues like nabidh al-jarr.

Core Idea
Ahl al-Sunnah defend the Companions' honor as a core principle, forbidding criticism even in disputed matters.

The author establishes a foundational principle of Ahl al-Sunnah: protecting the reputation of the Companions. This is not merely a polite preference but a doctrinal requirement tied to the transmission of faith. The passage explains why this is so and demonstrates its application through a concrete example involving Abu Hanifa's ruling on nabidh al-jarr.

Guided Path
1
Learn the principle
Read the opening declaration: 'among the principles of Ahl al-Sunnah is restraining the tongue from vilifying the Companions.' Notice that this is presented as a core tenet, not an optional stance.
2
Understand the justification
The author lists reasons: the Companions sacrificed themselves, transmitted the religion, and were honored by the Prophet's companionship. Reflect on how these reasons support the principle.
3
Examine the practical example
See how Abu Hanifa made avoiding the declaration of nabidh al-jarr as haram a condition of Sunnah. The logic: since many senior Companions drank it, declaring it haram would imply they were sinful, which contradicts the principle.
This shows the principle in action, not just theory.
4
Consider the objection and response
An opponent argues that by forbidding criticism, Sunnis ignore the conflicts among Sahaba. The author responds that those conflicts were based on ijtihad and error (khatā'), not sin. The passage ends before completing the response.
The response is cut off, but you can see the direction: attributing error, not sin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلموا أن من أصول مذهب السنة والجماعة كف اللسان عن الوقيعة في الصحابة، وحمل أمرهم على ما يوجب دفع الطعن والقدح عنهم؛ إذ هم الذين بذلوا أنفسهم وأموالهم، وودعوا الدعة والراحة، وتحملوا المشاق العظيمة في نصرة دين الله تعالى وهم نقلة الدين إلى من بعدهم وهم المكرمون بصحبة خير البشر - رحمه الله - ونصرته وإيوائه ووقايته بأنفسهم والجود بمُهَجهم دونه، ولهذا جعل أبو حنيفة من شرائط السنة أن لا يحرم نبيذ الجر لما أن في تحريمه تفسيق كبار الصحابة - رضي الله عنهم - لما ثبت بطريق لا شبهة فيه شربهم نبيذ الجر، ولو كان محرما لأوجب لك فسقهم، فكان القول بحرمته موجبا تفسيقهم، والقول بتفسيقهم بدعة وخروج عن شرائط مذهب أهل السنة والجماعة، فإن قالوا: إنكم تزعمون أن الوقيعة في الصحابة غير جائزة، ومن طعن فيهم واشتغل بالوقيعة فيهم فهو طعن البعض على البعض بل قصد رافضي، والصحابة - رضي الله عنهم البعض إراقة دم البعض فصار بعضهم فاسقا بذلك على زعمكم، فصرتم بمنعكم عن الطعن فيهم طاعنين فيهم وهو رفض عندكم فصرتم على زعمكم رافضة. قلنا لهم ما ذكرتم من طعن البعض في البعض فلم يكن إلا قدر نسبتهم إلى الخطأ فيما ذهبوا إليه بتأويلهم، وما روي أن البعض منهم فسق البعض بل أكثر ما
Then know that among the principles of the school of the Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah is restraining the tongue from vilification of the Companions, and interpreting their affair in a way that necessitates repelling criticism and defamation from them; for they are the ones who sacrificed themselves and their wealth, and abandoned ease and comfort, and bore great hardships in supporting the religion of Allah the Exalted, and they are the transmitters of the religion to those after them, and they are honored by the companionship of the best of mankind - may Allah have mercy on him - and by supporting him, giving him refuge, protecting him with themselves, and generously offering their lives for his sake. For this reason, Abu Hanifa made as a condition of the Sunnah that one not declare nabidh al-jarr forbidden, because declaring it forbidden would entail declaring senior Companions - may Allah be pleased with them - as sinners, since it is established by a means free of doubt that they drank nabidh al-jarr. And if it had been forbidden, that would have necessitated your declaring them sinners. So declaring it forbidden leads to declaring them sinners, and declaring them sinners is an innovation and a departure from the conditions of the school of the Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah. Now if they say: You claim that vilification of the Companions is not permissible, and whoever criticizes them and engages in vilification of them, then it is criticism of some by others, but rather a Rafidi intention. And the Companions - may Allah be pleased with them - some of them shed the blood of others, so some of them became sinners thereby according to your claim. So by your prohibition of criticizing them, you become critics of them, and that is Rafidism according to you. So you become, according to your claim, Rafidis. We say to them: What you mentioned of criticism of some by others did not occur except as attribution of them to error (khaṭa') in what they went to by way of their interpretation (ta'wīl). And it is not reported that some of them declared others sinners, but rather most of what...
Source pages p. 1184

Commentary
This passage is a statement of one of the foundational principles of Ahl al-Sunnah: refraining from speaking ill of the Companions (al-ṣaḥābah) and interpreting their actions in a favorable light to deflect criticism. The author gives the reasons: the Companions sacrificed themselves and their wealth, endured hardships for Islam, transmitted the religion, and are honored by the Prophet's companionship. He then provides a practical proof from Abu Hanifa, who included among the conditions of the Sunnah that one must not forbid nabidh al-jarr (a type of drink), because doing so would imply that many senior Companions who consumed it were sinful (fāsiq). Since the Companions cannot be declared sinful, forbidding nabidh al-jarr is itself an innovation (bid'ah). The author then addresses an objection: critics might say that by forbidding criticism, the Sunnis are actually criticizing the Companions because they ignore their internal conflicts where some shed blood. The author responds that these conflicts are attributable to error (khaṭa') in ijtihad, not to deliberate sin, and that no Companion actually declared another a sinner in those disputes. The passage ends mid-sentence, likely continuing the defense.

الوقيعة
الصحابة
نبيذ الجر
تفسيق
بدعة
الطعن
الخطأ
التأويل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ahl al-Sunnah demand that we never mention or discuss any conflict among the Sahaba, effectively ignoring them.
Correction: The author clarifies that Ahl al-Sunnah allow attributing error (khatā') based on ijtihad, not sin. So discussions about conflicts can occur but without accusing any Companion of deliberate sin.

Checkpoint
Why does Abu Hanifa forbid declaring nabidh al-jarr haram according to the passage?
A) Because it is clearly permitted in the Qur'an
B) Because declaring it haram would necessitate declaring senior Companions sinful
C) Because it is a matter of ijtihad
D) Because the Prophet specifically permitted it

Key Takeaway
The Sunni principle of refraining from vilifying the Companions is based on their sacrifices and role as transmitters, and is applied even to contentious rulings like nabidh al-jarr.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear someone criticize a Companion, what is your immediate reaction? Does your heart defend them, or do you stay silent? This lesson demands that you actively reframe their actions with a good opinion. Will you commit to doing that from now on?
?
The author uses a simple drink (nabidh) to illustrate a principle. Is there a contemporary issue where you have been quick to condemn without considering the implications for those who came before? What would it cost you to withhold judgment?
?
If you truly believe the Companions are the best generation, how does your daily speech reflect that belief? Do you gossip about scholars or pious predecessors? Take a moment to repent and set a concrete rule for your tongue.

Refraining from Disparaging the Companions and Defending Their Conflicts
Nasafi states the Sunni principle of refraining from disparaging the Companions and defends their conflicts as ijtihad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni principle of refraining from disparaging the Companions and the reasoning behind defending their internal disputes as matters of ijtihad.

Core Idea
The Companions' conflicts were ijtihad, not sin, so criticizing them breaches Sunni doctrine.

This passage from Nasafi establishes a cornerstone of Sunni Aqeedah: the obligation to protect the honor of the Companions. He not only states the principle but provides a concrete example (nabidh al-jarr) and responds to a common objection about the Companions' battles. As you read, notice how Nasafi uses both legal reasoning and theological principles to defend the Companions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the foundational principle
Read the opening lines where Nasafi states that restraining the tongue from disparaging the Companions is a fundamental of Ahl al-Sunnah. Note the reasons given: their sacrifices, transmission of religion, and companionship of the Prophet.
This principle is non-negotiable in Sunni Islam.
2
Examine the practical proof
Look at the example of nabidh al-jarr. See how Abu Hanifah made it a condition of Sunnah not to declare it prohibited, because that would imply the sinfulness of senior Companions who drank it.
This shows the care needed to avoid even indirect criticism of the Companions.
3
Analyze the objection and rebuttal
Read the objection: critics claim that because some Companions fought each other, Sunnis are inconsistent. Nasafi rebuts by explaining the conflicts were ijtihad, where each party was excused for error and no one declared sinners.
Note the quote from Ali: 'Our brothers have wronged us', not 'they are sinners'.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Nasafi warns that today, disparaging the Companions belittles the transmitters of religion and reveals hidden rancor, ultimately harming one's own faith.
This ties the principle to personal piety.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلموا أن من أصول مذهب السنة والجماعة كف اللسان عن الوقيعة في الصحابة، وحمل أمرهم على ما يوجب دفع الطعن والقدح عنهم؛ إذ هم الذين بذلوا أنفسهم وأموالهم، وودعوا الدعة والراحة، وتحملوا المشاق العظيمة في نصرة دين الله تعالى وهم نقلة الدين إلى من بعدهم وهم المكرمون بصحبة خير البشر ونصرته وإيوائه ووقايته بأنفسهم والجود بمُهجهم دونه، ولهذا جعل أبو حنيفة من شرائط السنة أن لا يحرم نبيذ الجر لما أن في تحريمه تفسيق كبار الصحابة - رضي الله عنهم - لما ثبت بطريق لا شبهة فيه شربهم نبيذ الجر، ولو كان محرما لأوجب لك فسقهم، فكان القول بحرمته موجبا تفسيقهم، والقول بتفسيقهم بدعة وخروج عن شرائط مذهب أهل السنة والجماعة، فإن قالوا: إنكم تزعمون أن الوقيعة في الصحابة غير جائزة، ومن طعن فيهم واشتغل بالوقيعة فيهم فهو طعن البعض على البعض بل قصد رافضي، والصحابة - رضي الله عنهم البعض إراقة دم البعض فصار بعضهم فاسقا بذلك على زعمكم، فصرتم بمنعكم عن الطعن فيهم طاعنين فيهم وهو رفض عندكم فصرتم على زعمكم رافضة. قلنا لهم ما ذكرتم من طعن البعض في البعض فلم يكن إلا قدر نسبتهم إلى الخطأ فيما ذهبوا إليه بتأويلهم، وما روي أن البعض منهم فسق البعض بل أكثر ما روي أن عليا قال فيهم : - إخواننا بغوا علينا - وكيف تفسقون وقد مر أنهم صاروا إلى ما صاروا مجتهدين، ثم الأصل في الاجتهاد أن كل أمر يحتمل العفو عنه والإباحة فيه، فمن أخطأ الحق في ذلك بالتأويل والاجتهاد فهو معذور، إذ بذل مجهوده وأنعم نظره فيما طمع أن يظفر فيه بالحق، وكذا ذكره الشيخ أبو منصور الماتريدي - رحمه الله - وكل خلاف كان بين الصحابة - رضي الله عنهم - كان من هذا القبيل إذ كان الله تعالى صان صحابة رسول الله عن اختلاف يوجب التضليل والتفسيق بفضله ورحمته، والقتال كان ليرتفع بالتباين ويعودوا إلى الألفة بعدما وقع بينهم من أسباب التضاغن عند انقطاع الطمع والرجاء عن العود إلى ذلك إلا بالقتال على ما قررنا والله المحمود. ولو ثبت من البعض أنه واجه غيره بخشن من القول فذلك على وجه التعزير له على قلة تأمله فيما يجتهد كما قال عمر لها الله لعبادة في أمر المثلث: يا أحمق، وللإمام إقامة التعزير بما يرى من المصلحة به، فأما اليوم فلا معنى لبسط اللسان فيهم إلا التهاون بنقلة الدين وناصريه، وإظهار ما أضمر الطاعن عن الحقد عليهم بسبب إظهارهم الدين الحق وقيامهم بنصرته وسعيهم في تمحيق الكفر والباطل، وقطع دابر أهله، فصار بالطعن فيهم مطعونًا في دينه، وبالله العصمة.
Then know that among the foundations of the school of Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah is restraining the tongue from disparaging the Companions, and interpreting their affairs in a manner that necessitates repelling criticism and discredit from them; for they are those who sacrificed themselves and their wealth, abandoned ease and comfort, and bore great hardships in supporting the religion of Allah the Exalted, and they are the transmitters of the religion to those after them, and they are honored by the companionship of the best of mankind, and by supporting him, giving him refuge, protecting him with their lives, and generously sacrificing their souls for him. For this reason, Abu Hanifah made it a condition of the Sunnah not to declare nabidh al-jarr prohibited, because declaring it prohibited would entail declaring the senior Companions - may Allah be pleased with them - to be sinners, since it is established without any doubt that they drank nabidh al-jarr. Had it been forbidden, it would necessarily entail their sinfulness, so declaring it prohibited necessitates declaring them sinners, and declaring them sinners is an innovation and a departure from the conditions of the school of Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah. Then they might say: 'You claim that disparaging the Companions is not permissible, and whoever criticizes them and occupies himself with disparaging them is criticizing one another, rather that is a Rafidi intention. The Companions - may Allah be pleased with them - some shed the blood of others, so some of them became sinners by that according to your claim. So by preventing criticism of them, you become critics of them, and that is Rafd according to you, so you become Rafidis according to your claim.' We say to them: What you mentioned of some criticizing others was nothing more than attributing them to error in what they undertook due to their interpretation. It is not reported that some of them declared others sinners; rather the most that has been reported is that 'Ali said about them: 'Our brothers have wronged us.' How can you declare them sinners when it has been stated that they arrived at what they did as mujtahids? The principle in ijtihad is that every matter is susceptible to forgiveness and permissibility. Whoever errs regarding the truth therein due to interpretation and ijtihad is excused, for he exerted his utmost effort and thoroughly examined his thinking in what he hoped to attain the truth in. This is what Shaykh Abu Mansur al-Maturidi - may Allah have mercy on him - mentioned. Every disagreement that occurred among the Companions - may Allah be pleased with them - was of this type, for Allah the Exalted protected the Companions of the Messenger of Allah from disagreement that would necessitate misguidance and sinfulness, by His grace and mercy. Fighting was intended to remove disparity and restore harmony after there had arisen among them causes of mutual hatred, when hope of returning to that other than through fighting was lost, as we have established. Allah is praised. If it is established that some of them addressed others with harsh words, that was by way of disciplinary rebuke for his lack of reflection in his ijtihad, as 'Umar said to 'Ubadah regarding the matter of the triangular: 'O foolish one!' The imam has the authority to administer disciplinary rebuke as he deems beneficial. As for today, there is no meaning to speaking freely about them except belittling the transmitters of the religion and its supporters, and revealing what the critic conceals of rancor against them because of their manifestation of the true religion, their standing in its support, and their striving to eradicate disbelief and falsehood and to cut off its followers. Thus, by criticizing them, one becomes critical of his own religion. With Allah is protection.
Source pages p. 1184

Commentary
This passage is a combined statement of principle, proof, objection, and rebuttal regarding the Sunni stance on the Companions. The author first states the foundational principle: it is obligatory to refrain from disparaging the Companions and to interpret their actions in a way that deflects criticism. He provides a practical proof: Abu Hanifah made it a condition of proper Sunnism not to declare nabidh al-jarr (a type of drink) prohibited, because doing so would imply that senior Companions, who drank it, were sinning, which is unacceptable. Then the author presents an objection from critics: the claim that some Companions fought each other, leading to mutual accusations of sin, so by forbidding criticism, the Sunnis themselves are implicitly criticizing them. The author responds by explaining that the conflicts were matters of ijtihad (independent reasoning), where each side sought truth and excused error. The fighters sought to resolve disputes, not to condemn each other as sinners. He cites 'Ali's statement 'our brothers have wronged us' as evidence of respect. He then adds that any harsh words were for disciplinary purposes, not condemnation. The passage concludes with a warning that today, disparaging the Companions reflects poorly on one's own religion.

Key Terms
الوقيعة في الصحابة
تفسيق الصحابة
الاجتهاد
نبيذ الجر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The internal conflicts among the Companions prove that some were sinners or misguided.
Correction: Nasafi argues that these conflicts were matters of ijtihad, where each party is excused for error, and no one declared the other a sinner. The fighting was to restore harmony, not due to sin.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did Abu Hanifah prohibit declaring nabidh al-jarr forbidden?
A) Because it was not mentioned in the Quran or Sunnah
B) Because declaring it forbidden would imply the sinfulness of senior Companions who drank it
C) Because it is explicitly permitted in the Hadith
D) Because it is a matter of consensus

Key Takeaway
The Companions' disagreements were matters of ijtihad, not sin; thus, it is obligatory to refrain from criticizing them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear or read about conflicts among the Companions, do you find yourself feeling unsettled or even judgmental? What specific steps will you take to train your heart to adopt the attitude of refraining from criticism taught in this lesson, especially when you discuss these events with others?
?
The passage warns that criticizing the Companions today amounts to criticizing your own religion. Is there any hidden criticism or subtle doubt you hold about any Companion? What will you do to purify your heart and align it with the reverence this lesson demands?
?
This lesson teaches that even harsh words between Companions, like 'Umar's comment to 'Ubadah, were disciplinary and not condemnatory. Think of a person in your life with whom you have exchanged harsh words. How can you now reinterpret that interaction in a more charitable light, following the spirit of this lesson, and what action will you take to restore harmony?

The Sunni Consensus on Abu Bakr's Superiority
Nasafi states the consensus and contrasts with the Rafidi view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni consensus on the superiority of Abu Bakr and how it distinguishes Sunni from Rafidi belief.

Core Idea
The best of this nation after the Prophet is Abu Bakr, according to Sunni consensus.

This lesson presents a clear doctrinal boundary between Ahl al-Sunnah and the Rafidah. Imam al-Nasafi states the unanimous agreement of the Sunnis that Abu Bakr al-Siddiq is the most excellent Companion, and contrasts it with the opposing claim of the Rafidah. Understanding this consensus is essential for recognizing a core tenet of Sunni theology regarding the ranking of the Companions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing. Then read the translation. Notice the definitive language used for the consensus and the contrast with the Rafidah.
The opening phrase 'الكلام في أن أبا بكر أفضل الصحابة' sets the topic.
2
Identify the two positions
The passage presents two opposing views: the unanimous position of Ahl al-Sunnah that Abu Bakr is the best, and the claim of the Rafidah that 'Ali is the best. Reflect on how this defines sectarian boundaries.
The term 'الروافض' refers to those who reject the legitimacy of the first three caliphs.
3
Reflect on the role of consensus
Notice that al-Nasafi uses 'أجمع' (are unanimous) to emphasize that this is not a matter of personal opinion but a binding agreement of the Sunni community. Ask yourself: why is consensus important in theological arguments?
Consensus (ijma') is a primary source of authority in Sunni Islam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في أن أبا بكر أفضل الصحابة] أجمع أهل السنة والجماعة على أن أفضل هذه الأمة بعد نبيها أبو بكر الصديق الله، فأما الروافض بأجمعهم فإنهم يزعمون أن أفضل الأمة علي الله
[Discussion that Abu Bakr is the best of the Companions] Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah are unanimous that the best of this nation after its Prophet is Abu Bakr al-Siddiq - may Allah be pleased with him. As for the Rafidah, all of them claim that the best of the nation is 'Ali - may Allah be pleased with him.
Source pages p. 1185

Commentary
This passage is a concise doctrinal statement. The author, al-Nasafi, declares the consensus (ijma') of Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah that Abu Bakr al-Siddiq is the most excellent of the entire Muslim community after the Prophet Muhammad. He then contrasts this with the position of the Rafidah (a term for Shi'a who reject the legitimacy of the first three caliphs), who hold that 'Ali is the best. The passage thus marks a clear boundary between Sunni and Shi'a belief on the ranking of the Companions.

Key Terms
أفضل الصحابة
أهل السنة والجماعة
الروافض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The best of the nation after the Prophet is 'Ali (as the Rafidah claim).
Correction: Ahl al-Sunnah hold that the best is Abu Bakr, and this is based on consensus.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consensus of Ahl al-Sunnah regarding the best of the nation after the Prophet?
A) It is 'Ali
B) It is Abu Bakr
C) It is 'Umar
D) There is no consensus

Key Takeaway
Ahl al-Sunnah are unanimous that Abu Bakr is the best of the nation after the Prophet, in contrast to the Rafidah who prefer 'Ali.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly and without hesitation affirm that Abu Bakr is the best of this ummah after the Prophet? If any part of you hesitates or leans toward another Companion, what does that reveal about the state of your heart toward the consensus of Ahl al-Sunnah? Will you actively seek to remove that doubt?
?
The Rafidah make a claim that contradicts this consensus. Have you ever felt sympathetic to their argument, even superficially? What would it mean for your standing in Ahl al-Sunnah if you secretly preferred Ali over Abu Bakr? Are you willing to investigate the proofs and submit to the truth, regardless of your emotions or background?
?
This lesson declares a consensus—a binding agreement of the scholars of the early community. If you truly believe this, does your daily life reflect that belief? Do you defend Abu Bakr's virtue when it is challenged, or do you remain silent? What is stopping you from making this conviction active in your speech and actions?

Sectarian Views on the Best of the Ummah
Enumeration of sectarian positions on the best of the Ummah after the Prophet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the spectrum of Islamic theological positions on who is the most excellent person after the Prophet, from the Sunni consensus to various Shi'i and Mu'tazili views.

Core Idea
Who is the best after the Prophet? Sunnis say Abu Bakr; others differ sharply.

The question of who is the most excellent (afdal) among the Companions was not merely theoretical—it shaped sectarian identities. In this passage, the author surveys the landscape, beginning with the Sunni consensus on Abu Bakr, then cataloging the positions of Shi'a subsects and Mu'tazila. This summary provides a clear map of how different groups answered this pivotal question.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Sunni position
Look for the phrase 'أجمع أهل السنة والجماعة' and note that they unanimously consider Abu Bakr al-Siddiq as the most excellent of the Ummah after the Prophet.
This consensus is the baseline against which all other views are compared.
2
Recognize the Rawafid and Imamiyya positions
Notice that the Rawafid (early Shi'a) claim Ali is best, while the Imamiyya (Twelvers) go further, holding that most Companions apostatized after the Prophet's death.
These groups reject the legitimacy of the first three caliphs.
3
Distinguish among Zaydi subsects
Compare the Jarudiyya (declare Abu Bakr a disbeliever), the Jiririyya (accept his caliphate and condemn the Jarudiyya), and the Ya'qubiyya (show loyalty to Abu Bakr and Umar but do not dissociate from those who curse them).
All three Zaydi groups ultimately prefer Ali over the other Companions.
4
Consider the Mu'tazili stance
Note that most later Mu'tazila (like al-Ka'bi) also prefer Ali, while al-Jubba'i suspends judgment pending evidence from a specific hadith.
This shows variation even within a single theological school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في أن أبا بكر أفضل الصحابة] أجمع أهل السنة والجماعة على أن أفضل هذه الأمة بعد نبيها أبو بكر الصديق، فأما الروافض بأجمعهم فإنهم يزعمون أن أفضل الأمة علي، فأما الإمامية فأكثرهم على أن من سوى علي وابنيه وفاطمة ونفر يسير من الصحابة ارتدوا بعد وفاة النبي عليه السلام، وكذا الجارودية من الزيدية يكفرون أبا بكر، فأما الجريرية من الزيدية وهم أصحاب سليمان بن جرير رؤساء الزيدية، فإنهم يثبتون إمامة أبي بكر وعمر رضي الله عنهما ويكفرون الجارودية منهم في إكفارهم أبا بكر وعمر، وكذا اليعقوبية من الزيدية يتولون أبا بكر وعمر غير أنهم لا يتبرءون ممن تبرأ من أبي بكر وعمر غير أن الجريرية واليعقوبية مع هذا يفضلون عليا على جميع الصحابة، وإلى هذا يذهب أكثر متأخري المعتزلة، ونص الكعبي على اختياره هذا المذهب في كتابه المسمى بعيون المسائل، وأما الجبائي من جملة رؤساء المعتزلة فإنه كان يتوقف في ذلك وكان يقول: إن صح خبر الطير فعلي أفضل.
Discussion that Abu Bakr is the best of the Companions: The Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a are in consensus that the best of this Ummah after its Prophet is Abu Bakr al-Siddiq. As for the Rawafid collectively, they claim that the best of the Ummah is Ali. As for the Imamiyya, most of them hold that besides Ali, his two sons, Fatima, and a few Companions, the rest apostatized after the death of the Prophet, peace be upon him. Likewise, the Jarudiyya of the Zaydiyya declare Abu Bakr a disbeliever. As for the Jiririyya of the Zaydiyya—they are the followers of Sulayman ibn Jarir, leaders of the Zaydiyya—they affirm the imamate of Abu Bakr and Umar, may Allah be pleased with them, and they declare the Jarudiyya among them disbelievers for their declaration of disbelief against Abu Bakr and Umar. Likewise, the Ya'qubiyya of the Zaydiyya show loyalty (yatawallawna) to Abu Bakr and Umar, but they do not dissociate from those who dissociate from Abu Bakr and Umar. However, the Jiririyya and Ya'qubiyya, despite this, prefer Ali over all the Companions. Most of the later Mu'tazila incline to this view, and al-Ka'bi explicitly stated his choice of this doctrine in his book called 'Uyun al-Masa'il. As for al-Jubba'i, one of the leaders of the Mu'tazila, he used to withhold judgment on this matter and said: 'If the hadith of the bird is authentic, then Ali is superior.'
Source pages p. 1185

Commentary
This passage is a classification and survey of sectarian views on the question of who is the most excellent (afdal) person in the Muslim community after the Prophet Muhammad. The author begins by stating the consensus of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a that Abu Bakr al-Siddiq holds that rank. He then lists the positions of various non-Sunni groups. The Rawafid (a term for the early Shi'a) claim Ali is the best. The Imamiyya (Twelver Shi'a) go further, holding that most Companions apostatized. The Jarudiyya (a Zaydi subsect) declare Abu Bakr a disbeliever. In contrast, the Jiririyya (another Zaydi group) accept the caliphates of Abu Bakr and Umar and condemn the Jarudiyya for their extremism. The Ya'qubiyya (also Zaydi) similarly respect Abu Bakr and Umar but do not dissociate from those who curse them, yet both Jiririyya and Ya'qubiyya still consider Ali superior to all other Companions. The author notes that most later Mu'tazila—including al-Ka'bi who wrote 'Uyun al-Masa'il—adopt this view (i.e., preferring Ali over the rest) although they accept Abu Bakr and Umar's caliphates. Al-Jubba'i, a major Mu'tazili figure, suspended judgment pending verification of the 'bird hadith' which would indicate Ali's superiority. The passage thus maps out the theological landscape on this pivotal issue of comparative merit among the Companions.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think all non-Sunni groups uniformly reject Abu Bakr.
Correction: The passage shows Zaydi subsects like Jiririyya and Ya'qubiyya accept his caliphate while still preferring Ali.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which Zaydi group declares Abu Bakr to be a disbeliever (kafir)?
A) Al-Jiririyya
B) Al-Ya'qubiyya
C) Al-Jarudiyya
D) Al-Imamiyya

Key Takeaway
The Sunni position is consensus among Sunnis, but other groups like the Rawafid, Zaydis, and Mu'tazila have varying views on Abu Bakr and Ali.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear different opinions about the Companions, do you examine the evidence yourself, or do you simply follow what your community says? What would it take for you to change your view if you found strong evidence against it?
?
The passage shows that some groups accept Abu Bakr's caliphate but still prefer Ali. Can you hold an honest difference in preference without letting it create division or hatred in your heart? How do you handle disagreements on matters of religious preference?
?
Al-Jubba'i suspended judgment on a matter because he needed verification of a hadith. Is there any issue in your faith where you are holding a position without having verified the evidence? Are you willing to say 'I don't know' when the evidence is unclear?

The Cave Verse as Proof for Abu Bakr's Superiority
The author presents the Quranic proof from Surah Tawbah for Abu Bakr's superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the verse of the cave (Surah Tawbah 9:40) is used as a scriptural proof for the superiority of Abu Bakr al-Siddiq.

Core Idea
The cave verse shows Abu Bakr as the Prophet's companion and recipient of divine calm.

This lesson examines a key proof from the Qur'an that Sunni scholars use to establish the superiority of Abu Bakr. The verse from Surah Tawbah describes the Hijra and the moment in the cave, highlighting Abu Bakr's closeness to the Prophet and Allah's special favor upon him. Let us see how the author explains this proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully, noting the verse citation and the author's beginning interpretation.
The text ends abruptly; the full conclusion is missing.
2
Identify key phrases
Look for phrases that indicate Abu Bakr's role: 'thāniya ithnayn' (second of two), 'ṣāḥibihī' (his companion), and 'lā taḥzan' (do not grieve).
3
Understand the interpretation
The author explains that the sakina (divine calm) was sent down upon Abu Bakr, as indicated by the pronoun 'alayhi' in the verse.
This shows Abu Bakr was honored directly by Allah.
4
Consider the incomplete argument
Note that the passage is cut off mid-sentence. The full proof would likely conclude that this verse establishes Abu Bakr's excellence over all other Companions.
The missing part may contain the final ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأدلة على أفضلية الصديق: إِلَّا تَنصُرُوهُ فَقَدْ نَصَرَهُ اللَّهُ إِذْ أَخْرَجَهُ الَّذِينَ كَفَرُوا ثَانِيَ اثْنَيْنِ إِذْ هُمَا فِي الْغَارِ إِذْ يَقُولُ لِصَاحِبِهِ لَا تَحْزَنْ إِنَّ اللَّهَ مَعَنَا فَأَنزَلَ اللَّهُ سَكِينَتَهُ عَلَيْهِ في الآية أنه صاحب رسول الله، وأن الله نصره كما نصر نبيه حيث قال "إن الله معنا" أي بالنصر، ثم الهاء في قوله: فأنزل الله سكينته عليه عائدة إلى
The proofs for the superiority of al-Siddiq: 'If you do not aid him, Allah has already aided him when those who disbelieved drove him out, the second of two, when they were in the cave, when he said to his companion, "Do not grieve; indeed Allah is with us." And Allah sent down His calm/sakina upon him.' In the verse, he is the companion of the Messenger of Allah, and that Allah aided him just as He aided His Prophet when he said 'Indeed Allah is with us' meaning with aid. Then the pronoun in His statement 'And Allah sent down His calm upon him' refers back to...
Source pages p. 1186

Commentary
This passage is a scriptural proof (dalil) for the superiority of Abu Bakr al-Siddiq, drawn from the Qur'anic verse 9:40 about the cave during the Hijra. The author cites the verse verbatim, highlighting that Allah describes Abu Bakr as 'the second of two' (thāniya ithnayn) and as the Prophet's 'companion' (ṣāḥibihī). The verse states that the Prophet said to him, 'Do not grieve; indeed Allah is with us.' The author explains that this indicates Allah aided Abu Bakr just as He aided the Prophet, and that the word 'us' implies divine support for both. The author then begins to interpret the pronoun 'upon him' (ʿalayhi) in 'Allah sent down His calm upon him' — noting that the pronoun refers back to (the companion), i.e., Abu Bakr, meaning the sakina (divine tranquility) was sent down specifically upon Abu Bakr, which is a mark of honor and virtue. However, the argument is cut off at this point. The full proof would conclude that this verse establishes Abu Bakr's excellence over all other Companions.

الآية
صاحبه
السكينة
ثاني اثنين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the verse only speaks about the Prophet, ignoring Abu Bakr's role.
Correction: The passage shows that the companion (Abu Bakr) is explicitly mentioned as the recipient of divine calm and the Prophet's reassurance, indicating his high rank.

Checkpoint

A) He was a prophet
B) He was the recipient of divine tranquility
C) He was the one who drove the Prophet out
D) He was the speaker in the cave

Key Takeaway
The cave verse provides a direct scriptural evidence that Abu Bakr was honored by Allah with divine calm and was the Prophet's chosen companion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that Allah sent down His calm upon Abu Bakr in that moment of fear, do you truly believe that Allah can send calm into your own heart during your most anxious trials? What would it take for you to trust that divine presence as Abu Bakr did?
?
The Prophet reassured Abu Bakr with 'Allah is with us.' Have you ever felt the weight of being alone in a struggle? How can you actively remind yourself that Allah is with you, not just as a thinker but as a living conviction that changes your response to fear?
?
If Allah chose to honor Abu Bakr with such a clear sign of favor, does your heart accept divine choices without resentment or comparison? Examine yourself: are there people in your life whom Allah has clearly blessed, and do you truly rejoice for them, or does envy creep in?
