A) By pointing out that the hadith is weak.
B) By showing that similar praises for others do not make them absolutely superior.
C) By claiming that Ali was not actually the best judge.
D) By arguing that the hadith only applied during the Prophet's lifetime.

Key Takeaway
Specific praiseworthy qualities among the Companions do not confer overall superiority; each Companion has his own distinction, and claiming otherwise leads to contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of someone you consider 'lesser' than yourself in one area—maybe they are less knowledgeable or less skilled. How does this lesson challenge you to reconsider your overall judgment of them as a person? What specific change in attitude will you make today?
?

?
The Companions each had unique virtues, and no single one was superior in all respects. How can you apply this lesson to your own community—recognizing that each person has a distinct role without ranking some as inherently better? Name one person you will affirm this week for their unique contribution, and what will you say?

True Bravery: Redefining Courage in the Sahabah
Nasafi refutes the claim that Ali was the bravest, citing Abu Bakr's courage in critical events.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that true bravery in Islamic theology is defined by composure and courage of the heart, not physical strength, and how Abu Bakr exemplified this in critical moments.

Core Idea
True bravery is composure in crisis, not brute strength.

This passage directly challenges a common sectarian claim by redefining bravery itself. Before evaluating who was brave, we must ask: what is bravery? The author argues that it lies in composure and disregard for danger, and then demonstrates how Abu Bakr's actions after the Prophet's death epitomize this quality.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the author's distinction between physical strength and true bravery.
The author begins with 'ونقول' indicating a direct response.
2
Identify the criteria for bravery
Find the phrase that defines true virtue: 'الرباطة الجأش، وشجاعة القلب، وترك الاكتراث بالمهالك'.
These three qualities are the essence of bravery according to the author.
3
Analyze Abu Bakr's actions
Notice the series of events after the Prophet's death illustrating his steadfastness and resolve: his calmness, his speech, his leadership, and his determination against apostates.
The author emphasizes 'ثابت الجنان' and 'لم يتحير'.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider how this evidence supports the refutation and challenges the initial claim that Ali was the bravest.
The evidence is presented as a counterexample showing superior bravery.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودعواهم أنه أشجع الناس فنقول: إن زيادة قوة في البدن ليست فيها زيادة فضيلة، إنما الفضيلة في الرباطة الجأش، وشجاعة القلب، وترك الاكتراث بالمهالك، ولم يكن أحد في هذه المعاني مساويا لأبي بكر الصديق رضي الله عنه؛ ولهذا لم يتحير في أمره ولم يطر قلبه كل مطار عندما هجمت عليه المعضلة الصماء والخطة الدهماء بفوات نبي الرحمة، ثم خرج ثابت الجنان قوي اللسان نافذ البصيرة شديد الشكيمة فصرح بوفاة النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم، ودعاهم إلى الاعتصام بحبل الله المتين فقال: ألا إن من كان يعبد محمدا فإن محمدا قد مات ومن كان يعبد رب محمد فإن رب محمد حي لا يموت ثم تقلد إمامة الأمة، واستأنف الأمور بالجد والصرامة، فنفذ جيش أسامة رضي الله عنه وأرسل الجيوش إلى المرتدين ومانعي الزكاة غير مكترث بكثرة الأعداء، ولا مبال من اجتماعهم على التظاهر والتناصر والتحزب والتآلب، فقال: والله لو منعوني عقالا مما كانوا يؤدونه إلى رسول الله لقاتلتهم وغيره من الصحابة.
And their claim that he was the bravest of people—we say: Increase of bodily strength does not entail increase of virtue; virtue lies in composure, courage of the heart, and disregard for dangers. And no one was equal to Abu Bakr al-Siddiq (may Allah be pleased with him) in these meanings. For this reason, he was not bewildered in his affair, nor did his heart flutter when the overwhelming calamity and the dark ordeal befell him due to the passing of the Prophet of mercy. Then he came forth with firm heart, strong tongue, penetrating insight, and intense determination, and declared the death of the Prophet (peace be upon him), and called them to hold fast to the strong rope of Allah, saying: 'Indeed, whoever used to worship Muhammad, then Muhammad has died; and whoever used to worship the Lord of Muhammad, then the Lord of Muhammad is alive and does not die.' Then he undertook the leadership of the community, and resumed affairs with seriousness and rigor. He dispatched the army of Usama (may Allah be pleased with him) and sent armies against the apostates and those who withheld zakat, not caring about the multitude of enemies, nor paying attention to their gathering in display, mutual support, partisanship, and alliance. He said: 'By Allah, if they were to withhold from me a hobbling cord of what they used to give to the Messenger of Allah, I would fight them for it.' And other Companions...
Source pages p. 1197

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that Ali was the bravest person. The author distinguishes between physical strength and true bravery, which consists of composure, courage of heart, and disregard for dangers. He asserts that no one matched Abu Bakr in these qualities. As evidence, the author recounts Abu Bakr's actions after the Prophet's death: he remained calm, delivered a firm speech affirming the Prophet's death and calling people to hold fast to Allah, then took on the caliphate, executed Usama's army, fought apostates and zakat refusers with resolve. His statement 'If they withhold a hobbling cord...' demonstrates his unwavering commitment. The passage argues that such bravery surpasses mere physical courage.

Key Terms
رباطة الجأش
شجاعة القلب
ثابت الجنان
الشجاعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some equate bravery with physical strength or martial prowess.
Correction: The author shows that true bravery is about composure, courage of the heart, and disregard for dangers, as exemplified by Abu Bakr.

Checkpoint

A) Physical strength and skill in combat.
B) Composure, courage of the heart, and disregard for dangers.
C) The number of battles fought.
D) The number of enemies killed.

Key Takeaway
True bravery is measured by composure and courage in the face of crisis, not physical strength, as exemplified by Abu Bakr after the Prophet's death.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The Companions were many, yet Abu Bakr stood alone with his conviction and acted decisively. Where in your life are you remaining silent or passive because you fear the disapproval of others? Are you willing to stand for truth even if you stand alone?

The 'Best Judge' Hadith Does Not Prove Superiority
Author refutes the 'best judge' hadith argument for Ali's superiority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Prophet's statement 'The best judge among you is Ali' does not establish Ali's overall superiority over Abu Bakr.

Core Idea
A single virtue does not make someone the best overall.

One argument for Ali's superiority is the Prophet's saying that Ali is the best judge. But the author points out that the Prophet also praised other Companions in specific domains without making them superior overall. This lesson examines that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage where the author addresses the 'best judge' claim and the translation. Notice how the author uses analogy with other hadiths.
2
Identify the argument and refutation
The claim: 'Ali is the best judge' implies he is superior. The refutation: The Prophet also said 'The best reciter is Abu Bakr' and 'The most knowledgeable in inheritance is Zaid' without making Zaid infallible. So the same applies to Ali's judgment.
The author shows that a specific virtue does not imply absolute superiority.
3
Reflect on the principle
Think about why this reasoning is important for maintaining harmony among the Companions. The author says each Companion has unique virtues, but claiming superiority from one virtue would lead to contradiction.
This principle protects against sectarian bias.

ولا تعلق لهم أيضا بقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: "أقضاكم علي" فإنه عليه السلام قال أيضا : أقرؤكم أبو بكر ، وأفرضكم زيد ولم يكن ذلك دليلا أن زيدا هو المصيب في الفرائض وغيره مبطل، فكذا هذا في القضاء، ثم كل من الصحابة كانت له فضائل جمة لا يدرك قعرها، ولكل منهم خصوصية لا يشاركه فيها غيره، ولا كلام في ذلك، وذلك لا يوجب أن من اختص بفضيلة كان أفضل من غيره من الصحابة لما في ذلك من إثبات التناقض، والله الموفق.
ودعواهم أنه أشجع الناس فنقول : إن زيادة قوة في البدن ليست فيها زيادة فضيلة، إنما الفضيلة الرباطة الجأش ، وشجاعة القلب، وترك الاكتراث بالمهالك، ولم يكن أحد في هذه المعاني مساويا لأبي بكر الصديق رضي الله عنه؛ ولهذا لم يتحير في أمره ولم يطر قلبه كل مطار عندما هجمت عليه المعضلة الصماء والخطة الدهماء بفوات نبي الرحمة، ثم خرج ثابت الجنان قوي اللسان نافذ البصيرة شديد الشكيمة فصرح بوفاة النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم، ودعاهم ( ) إلى الاعتصام بحبل الله المتين فقال: "ألا إن من كان يعبد محمدا فإن محمدا قد مات ومن كان يعبد رب محمد فإن رب محمد حي لا يموت" ثم تقلد إمامة الأمة، واستأنف الأمور بالجد والصرامة، فنفذ جيش أسامة رضي الله عنه وأرسل الجيوش إلى المرتدين ومانعي الزكاة غير مكترث بكثرة الأعداء، ولا مبال من اجتماعهم على التظاهر والتناصر والتحزب والتآلب، فقال: والله لو منعوني عقالا مما كانوا يؤدونه إلى رسول الله لقاتلتهم عليه وغيره من الصحابة رضي الله عنهم كانوا يغتنمون السلامة، ويكتفون بأن ينجو من شر الأعداء رأسا برأس، وكانوا يدعونه إلى ذلك ويشيرون له إلى ترك التعرض لهم والإعراض عنهم إلى أن تتهيأ لهم الأسباب وتنفتح عليهم إليه الأبواب، فأبت حميته في الدين، وصلابته في الإسلام وشدة توكله على ما وعد الله تعالى في كتابه، وعلى لسان رسوله الصادق المصدق من إظهار الدين ونشر الدعوة إلا التصميم على ما عزم والإصرار على ما استصوب، وهذه والله الشجاعة المحمودة والبسالة المرضية، فكان الدعوى - أن غيره أشجع منه قلبا وأربط منه جاشا وأحمى ذمارا وأقل مبالاة من المخاوف وأشد منه اقتحاما في المتالف - دعوى مرفوضة ممنوعة لا يساعدها الدليل، ولا يعاضدها البرهان.
وما تزعمون أن عليا رضي الله عنه نام على فراش النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم مع علمه بقصد الكفار ولم يخف، وأبو بكر كان يحزن في الغار . ثم نقول لهم : إن أبا بكر إنما كان يحزن لأجل رسول الله عليه السلام لا لأجل نفسه.
ألا يرى كيف فداه نفسه بإلقامه رجله الحية، ثم إن عليا لم يخف لأن النبي عليه السلام كان أخبرهم أنهم لا يصلون إليه، ولو أخبر النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم بذلك لواحد منا لا تحزن إن الله معنا لم يخف فلم يثبت بذلك زيادة شجاعة من علي.
وأما تعلقهم بكثرة من قتله علي من فرسان العرب الذين اشتهر عنادهم وكثر في الأعداء نكايتهم فلقد كان ذلك لا يجحد له جاحد غير أن من قتل علي بيديه وصار إلى النار لا يبلغ جزءا قليلا ممن استنقذهم الله تعالى ببركة أبي بكر من النيران
They have no basis either in his statement, peace and blessings be upon him: 'The best judge among you is Ali,' for he also said, 'The best reciter among you is Abu Bakr, and the most knowledgeable in inheritance among you is Zaid.' That does not prove that Zaid is correct in inheritance matters and others are wrong; so likewise this (saying) is about judgment. Then, each of the Companions had abundant virtues whose depths cannot be grasped, and each of them has a specificity that no other shares, and there is no argument about that. But that does not require that the one distinguished by a virtue is better than other Companions, because that would entail contradiction. And God is the grantor of success. And their claim that he (Ali) was the bravest of people – we say: Increase in bodily strength does not increase virtue; rather, virtue lies in steadfastness of composure, courage of the heart, and disregard for perils. And no one was equal to Abu Bakr al-Siddiq, may God be pleased with him, in these meanings. For this reason, he did not become confused in his affair nor was his heart agitated in every direction when the tremendous calamity and the awful misfortune befell him due to the passing of the Prophet of mercy. Then he emerged with firm heart, strong tongue, penetrating insight, and intense resoluteness, and he openly declared the death of the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him, and called them to hold fast to the firm rope of God, saying: 'Indeed, whoever used to worship Muhammad, Muhammad has died; and whoever used to worship the Lord of Muhammad, the Lord of Muhammad is Living and does not die.' Then he undertook the leadership of the community, and resumed affairs with seriousness and rigor. He dispatched the army of Usama, may God be pleased with him, and sent armies against the apostates and those who withheld zakah, not caring about the multitude of enemies, nor concerned about their gathering to show off, support each other, form parties, and unite. He said: 'By God, if they were to withhold from me a hobbling-cord of what they used to pay to the Messenger of God, I would fight them over it.' Other Companions, may God be pleased with them, would seize safety, and be content to escape from the evil of enemies head to head, and they invited him to that and indicated to him to leave them alone and turn away from them until circumstances become favorable and doors open for him. But his zeal for religion, his firmness in Islam, and his strong trust in what God Almighty promised in His Book and on the tongue of His truthful and believed Messenger regarding the manifestation of the religion and spread of the call, refused (anything) except determination on what he resolved and persistence on what he deemed correct. These, by God, are praiseworthy courage and approved valor. So the claim – that another is more courageous in heart, more steadfast in composure, more protective of honor, less caring of fears, and more daring in plunging into destruction – is a rejected and prohibited claim not supported by evidence, nor sustained by proof. And as for your assertion that Ali, may God be pleased with him, slept on the bed of the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him, knowing the intention of the disbelievers, and was not afraid, while Abu Bakr was sad in the cave – then we say to them: Abu Bakr was sad only for the sake of the Messenger of God, peace be upon him, not for himself. Do you not see how he sacrificed himself by placing his foot in the snake hole? Moreover, Ali was not afraid because the Prophet, peace be upon him, had informed them that they would not reach him. And if the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him, had informed anyone of us of that – 'Do not be sad, indeed God is with us' – he would not have feared. So that does not prove any increase in courage for Ali. And as for their clinging to the large number of those whom Ali killed among the knights of the Arabs who were famous for their obstinacy and whose harm among the enemies was great – indeed that is not denied by any denier. However, those whom Ali killed with his own hands and who went to the Fire do not amount to a small portion of those whom God Almighty saved from the Fire through the blessing of Abu Bakr.
Source pages p. 1197

Commentary
This passage is a sustained refutation by the author of various claims made by those who assert the superiority of Ali over Abu Bakr. The author addresses several arguments in order. First, he rejects the argument based on the Prophet's statement 'The best judge among you is Ali' by analogy: the Prophet also said 'The best reciter Abu Bakr' and 'The most knowledgeable in inheritance Zaid' without that making Zaid infallible in all inheritance rulings. So the statement about Ali does not prove his absolute superiority. Second, he refutes the claim that Ali was the bravest of people. The author distinguishes bodily strength from true courage, which is steadfastness of heart and disregard for perils. He argues that Abu Bakr exemplified this in his unwavering response to the Prophet's death, his firm address to the Companions, and his decisive actions against the apostates and those withholding zakah despite the odds and contrary advice. The author describes Abu Bakr's bravery as praiseworthy and approved, and dismisses the counterclaim as unsupported. Third, he addresses the argument that Ali's sleeping in the Prophet's bed shows greater courage than Abu Bakr's sadness in the cave. The author explains that Abu Bakr's sadness was for the Prophet, not fear for himself, and that Ali's fearlessness was due to prior knowledge of safety from the Prophet. Finally, he considers the argument about Ali's many kills in battle. While acknowledging Ali's martial prowess, the author asserts that the number of people he killed who went to Hell is insignificant compared to the multitude saved from Hellfire through Abu Bakr's guidance and leadership. Throughout, the author maintains that each Companion has unique virtues but none entail overall superiority, as that would contradict the harmony among them.

Key Terms
أقضاكم
أقرؤكم
أفرضكم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Prophet's praise of Ali as 'best judge' proves Ali is better than all other Companions.
Correction: The Prophet also praised other Companions in specific domains, but that does not make them superior overall; each Companion has unique virtues without one being better than another.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the argument that Ali is superior because he was called the best judge?
A) Because the hadith is weak
B) Because the Prophet also praised Abu Bakr as best reciter and Zaid as most knowledgeable in inheritance, showing that specific praise does not mean overall superiority
C) Because Ali himself rejected this claim
D) Because the author does not accept the hadith

Key Takeaway
A specific virtue or skill mentioned in a hadith does not prove that the person is superior to all others; each Companion has unique merits.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have likely ranked people you know based on one impressive skill. Whom have you unfairly dismissed because they lacked a quality you value? What would it take for you to see their complete personhood as this lesson teaches us to see the Companions?
?
The Prophet praised multiple Companions for different strengths. Is there someone in your life whose strengths you ignore because you are focused on their one weakness? Write down one specific step you will take this week to acknowledge that person's virtue.
?
This lesson reveals that claiming overall superiority from a single virtue leads to contradiction. Where in your own thinking do you contradict yourself by holding someone to a standard you don't apply to others? Repent and resolve to be consistent.

Sects and Disagreements
beginner
3 min
True Bravery is Courage of Heart, Not Bodily Strength
Author redefines bravery as heart-centered virtue

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between bodily strength and true courage, and why the latter is the measure of virtue.

Core Idea
Real bravery is steadfastness of the heart and disregard for dangers.

Some claim Ali was braver than Abu Bakr because of his physical prowess and battles. The author counters by defining true bravery as composure and courage of the heart, not mere bodily strength. This lesson unpacks that definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage defining true courage
Read the Arabic and translation where the author says: 'Increase in bodily strength does not increase virtue; rather, virtue lies in steadfastness of composure, courage of the heart, and disregard for perils.'
2
Analyze the definition
The author identifies three elements: steadfastness (رباطة الجأش), courage of the heart (شجاعة القلب), and disregard for perils (ترك الاكتراث بالمهالك). Note that these are internal qualities, not physical.
This contrasts with the common association of bravery with physical strength.
3
Reflect on the implication
If true bravery is internal, then it is not determined by how many enemies one kills or how strong one is physically. The author will later apply this to Abu Bakr.

According to the author, what is the real basis of virtue in bravery?
A) Bodily strength and skill in combat
B) Steadfastness of composure, courage of heart, and disregard for perils
C) Number of enemies killed
D) Fame and recognition

Key Takeaway
True bravery is defined by inner composure and courage, not physical ability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you more concerned with appearing strong to others or being inwardly steadfast when trials come? What situation right now is testing your composure (رباطة الجأش)? How will you strengthen it starting today?
?
When was the last time you had to disregard your own comfort or reputation for a principle of faith? Are you brave enough to ignore what people think and act solely for Allah? Name one fear you need to overcome by tomorrow.
?
Abu Bakr's bravery was in his heart, not his body. Look at your own heart: what fears are holding you back from obeying Allah fully? Write down one step you will take today to confront that fear head-on.

Abu Bakr's Steadfastness at the Prophet's Death
Author demonstrates Abu Bakr's bravery through his actions after the Prophet's death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will see how Abu Bakr's response to the Prophet's death exemplifies true bravery and why the author considers him unmatched in this regard.

Core Idea
Abu Bakr's calm and decisive action during the crisis of the Prophet's death proves his unparalleled courage.

After defining true bravery, the author presents Abu Bakr's conduct after the Prophet's death as its embodiment. While others were stunned, Abu Bakr remained composed, affirmed the Prophet's death, and took decisive leadership. This lesson examines that example.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on Abu Bakr's actions
Read the Arabic and translation describing Abu Bakr's behavior when the Prophet died: he did not become confused, he addressed the people with a firm speech, and he took charge.
2
Identify the elements of true bravery in his actions
Notice phrases: 'لم يتحير في أمره' (he did not become confused), 'ثابت الجنان' (firm heart), 'قوي اللسان' (strong tongue), 'نافذ البصيرة' (penetrating insight), 'شديد الشكيمة' (intense resoluteness). These match the definition from Lesson 2.
3
Reflect on the contrast with others
The author notes that other Companions were inclined to seek safety and avoid conflict, but Abu Bakr's 'حميته في الدين' (zeal for religion) and 'صلابته في الإسلام' (firmness in Islam) drove him to act decisively against apostates and those withholding zakat.
This contrast emphasizes his unique courage.

ولا تعلق لهم أيضا بقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: "أقضاكم علي" فإنه عليه السلام قال أيضا : أقرؤكم أبو بكر ، وأفرضكم زيد ولم يكن ذلك دليلا أن زيدا هو المصيب في الفرائض وغيره مبطل، فكذا هذا في القضاء، ثم كل من الصحابة كانت له فضائل جمة لا يدرك قعرها، ولكل منهم خصوصية لا يشاركه فيها غيره، ولا كلام في ذلك، وذلك لا يوجب أن من اختص بفضيلة كان أفضل من غيره من الصحابة لما في ذلك من إثبات التناقض، والله الموفق.
ودعواهم أنه أشجع الناس فنقول : إن زيادة قوة في البدن ليست فيها زيادة فضيلة، إنما الفضيلة الرباطة الجأش ، وشجاعة القلب، وترك الاكتراث بالمهالك، ولم يكن أحد في هذه المعاني مساويا لأبي بكر الصديق رضي الله عنه؛ ولهذا لم يتحير في أمره ولم يطر قلبه كل مطار عندما هجمت عليه المعضلة الصماء والخطة الدهماء بفوات نبي الرحمة، ثم خرج ثابت الجنان قوي اللسان نافذ البصيرة شديد الشكيمة فصرح بوفاة النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم، ودعاهم ( ) إلى الاعتصام بحبل الله المتين فقال: "ألا إن من كان يعبد محمدا فإن محمدا قد مات ومن كان يعبد رب محمد فإن رب محمد حي لا يموت" ثم تقلد إمامة الأمة، واستأنف الأمور بالجد والصرامة، فنفذ جيش أسامة رضي الله عنه وأرسل الجيوش إلى المرتدين ومانعي الزكاة غير مكترث بكثرة الأعداء، ولا مبال من اجتماعهم على التظاهر والتناصر والتحزب والتآلب، فقال: والله لو منعوني عقالا مما كانوا يؤدونه إلى رسول الله لقاتلتهم عليه وغيره من الصحابة رضي الله عنهم كانوا يغتنمون السلامة، ويكتفون بأن ينجو من شر الأعداء رأسا برأس، وكانوا يدعونه إلى ذلك ويشيرون له إلى ترك التعرض لهم والإعراض عنهم إلى أن تتهيأ لهم الأسباب وتنفتح عليهم إليه الأبواب، فأبت حميته في الدين، وصلابته في الإسلام وشدة توكله على ما وعد الله تعالى في كتابه، وعلى لسان رسوله الصادق المصدق من إظهار الدين ونشر الدعوة إلا التصميم على ما عزم والإصرار على ما استصوب، وهذه والله الشجاعة المحمودة والبسالة المرضية، فكان الدعوى - أن غيره أشجع منه قلبا وأربط منه جاشا وأحمى ذمارا وأقل مبالاة من المخاوف وأشد منه اقتحاما في المتالف - دعوى مرفوضة ممنوعة لا يساعدها الدليل، ولا يعاضدها البرهان.
وما تزعمون أن عليا رضي الله عنه نام على فراش النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم مع علمه بقصد الكفار ولم يخف، وأبو بكر كان يحزن في الغار . ثم نقول لهم : إن أبا بكر إنما كان يحزن لأجل رسول الله عليه السلام لا لأجل نفسه.
ألا يرى كيف فداه نفسه بإلقامه رجله الحية، ثم إن عليا لم يخف لأن النبي عليه السلام كان أخبرهم أنهم لا يصلون إليه، ولو أخبر النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم بذلك لواحد منا لا تحزن إن الله معنا لم يخف فلم يثبت بذلك زيادة شجاعة من علي.
وأما تعلقهم بكثرة من قتله علي من فرسان العرب الذين اشتهر عنادهم وكثر في الأعداء نكايتهم فلقد كان ذلك لا يجحد له جاحد غير أن من قتل علي بيديه وصار إلى النار لا يبلغ جزءا قليلا ممن استنقذهم الله تعالى ببركة أبي بكر من النيران
They have no basis either in his statement, peace and blessings be upon him: 'The best judge among you is Ali,' for he also said, 'The best reciter among you is Abu Bakr, and the most knowledgeable in inheritance among you is Zaid.' That does not prove that Zaid is correct in inheritance matters and others are wrong; so likewise this (saying) is about judgment. Then, each of the Companions had abundant virtues whose depths cannot be grasped, and each of them has a specificity that no other shares, and there is no argument about that. But that does not require that the one distinguished by a virtue is better than other Companions, because that would entail contradiction. And God is the grantor of success. And their claim that he (Ali) was the bravest of people – we say: Increase in bodily strength does not increase virtue; rather, virtue lies in steadfastness of composure, courage of the heart, and disregard for perils. And no one was equal to Abu Bakr al-Siddiq, may God be pleased with him, in these meanings. For this reason, he did not become confused in his affair nor was his heart agitated in every direction when the tremendous calamity and the awful misfortune befell him due to the passing of the Prophet of mercy. Then he emerged with firm heart, strong tongue, penetrating insight, and intense resoluteness, and he openly declared the death of the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him, and called them to hold fast to the firm rope of God, saying: 'Indeed, whoever used to worship Muhammad, Muhammad has died; and whoever used to worship the Lord of Muhammad, the Lord of Muhammad is Living and does not die.' Then he undertook the leadership of the community, and resumed affairs with seriousness and rigor. He dispatched the army of Usama, may God be pleased with him, and sent armies against the apostates and those who withheld zakah, not caring about the multitude of enemies, nor concerned about their gathering to show off, support each other, form parties, and unite. He said: 'By God, if they were to withhold from me a hobbling-cord of what they used to pay to the Messenger of God, I would fight them over it.' Other Companions, may God be pleased with them, would seize safety, and be content to escape from the evil of enemies head to head, and they invited him to that and indicated to him to leave them alone and turn away from them until circumstances become favorable and doors open for him. But his zeal for religion, his firmness in Islam, and his strong trust in what God Almighty promised in His Book and on the tongue of His truthful and believed Messenger regarding the manifestation of the religion and spread of the call, refused (anything) except determination on what he resolved and persistence on what he deemed correct. These, by God, are praiseworthy courage and approved valor. So the claim – that another is more courageous in heart, more steadfast in composure, more protective of honor, less caring of fears, and more daring in plunging into destruction – is a rejected and prohibited claim not supported by evidence, nor sustained by proof. And as for your assertion that Ali, may God be pleased with him, slept on the bed of the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him, knowing the intention of the disbelievers, and was not afraid, while Abu Bakr was sad in the cave – then we say to them: Abu Bakr was sad only for the sake of the Messenger of God, peace be upon him, not for himself. Do you not see how he sacrificed himself by placing his foot in the snake hole? Moreover, Ali was not afraid because the Prophet, peace be upon him, had informed them that they would not reach him. And if the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him, had informed anyone of us of that – 'Do not be sad, indeed God is with us' – he would not have feared. So that does not prove any increase in courage for Ali. And as for their clinging to the large number of those whom Ali killed among the knights of the Arabs who were famous for their obstinacy and whose harm among the enemies was great – indeed that is not denied by any denier. However, those whom Ali killed with his own hands and who went to the Fire do not amount to a small portion of those whom God Almighty saved from the Fire through the blessing of Abu Bakr.
Source pages p. 1197

Commentary
This passage is a sustained refutation by the author of various claims made by those who assert the superiority of Ali over Abu Bakr. The author addresses several arguments in order. First, he rejects the argument based on the Prophet's statement 'The best judge among you is Ali' by analogy: the Prophet also said 'The best reciter Abu Bakr' and 'The most knowledgeable in inheritance Zaid' without that making Zaid infallible in all inheritance rulings. So the statement about Ali does not prove his absolute superiority. Second, he refutes the claim that Ali was the bravest of people. The author distinguishes bodily strength from true courage, which is steadfastness of heart and disregard for perils. He argues that Abu Bakr exemplified this in his unwavering response to the Prophet's death, his firm address to the Companions, and his decisive actions against the apostates and those withholding zakah despite the odds and contrary advice. The author describes Abu Bakr's bravery as praiseworthy and approved, and dismisses the counterclaim as unsupported. Third, he addresses the argument that Ali's sleeping in the Prophet's bed shows greater courage than Abu Bakr's sadness in the cave. The author explains that Abu Bakr's sadness was for the Prophet, not fear for himself, and that Ali's fearlessness was due to prior knowledge of safety from the Prophet. Finally, he considers the argument about Ali's many kills in battle. While acknowledging Ali's martial prowess, the author asserts that the number of people he killed who went to Hell is insignificant compared to the multitude saved from Hellfire through Abu Bakr's guidance and leadership. Throughout, the author maintains that each Companion has unique virtues but none entail overall superiority, as that would contradict the harmony among them.

المعضلة الصماء
الخطة الدهماء
ثابت الجنان
شكيمة
الحمية في الدين
التوكل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What was Abu Bakr's immediate response to the Prophet's death?
A) He mourned quietly and withdrew
B) He became confused and sought advice
C) He remained composed, declared the Prophet's death, and gave a speech to the people
D) He called for a military campaign against the apostates immediately

Key Takeaway
Abu Bakr's calm and decisive leadership in the crisis of the Prophet's death demonstrates true courage as defined by the author.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a crisis that shakes the foundations of your faith – like the death of a prophet for the Companions – do you respond with the calm resolve of Abu Bakr, or do you become agitated and seek the safety of inaction? What is the one step you will take today to strengthen your trust in God's promises so that you can act decisively for His sake?
?
Abu Bakr refused to wait for favorable conditions and fought against overwhelming odds because of his zeal for religion. Is there an area in your life where you are hesitating out of fear, playing it safe, while the deen calls you to act? What is that area, and when will you take the first step of courage?
?
The author counts Abu Bakr's actions as 'praiseworthy courage'. If someone examined your life today, what specific evidence would they find of your courage in faith? Are you more like Abu Bakr, who acted despite the multitude, or like those who 'seize safety'? Ask yourself honestly: what has your fear cost you in your journey to Allah?

Sects and Disagreements
intermediate
4 min
The Bed vs. Cave Incident: A Reassessment of Bravery
Author refutes the argument comparing Ali's bed-sleeping to Abu Bakr's cave fear

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author considers the claim that Ali's sleeping in the Prophet's bed proves greater bravery than Abu Bakr's sadness in the cave to be invalid.

Core Idea
Abu Bakr's fear was for the Prophet, not himself, and Ali's fearlessness was due to divine protection promised.

A common argument for Ali's courage is his willingness to sleep in the Prophet's bed while the Quraysh plotted to kill him, contrasted with Abu Bakr's fear in the cave. The author responds by examining motives and context. This lesson explores that rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the bed and cave
Read the Arabic and translation where the author addresses the claim that Ali's sleeping in the bed showed more courage than Abu Bakr's sadness in the cave.
2
Analyze Abu Bakr's sadness
The author says Abu Bakr was sad for the sake of the Prophet, not for himself. Note the example of him putting his foot in the snake hole to protect the Prophet.
This shows selflessness, not cowardice.
3
Analyze Ali's fearlessness
The author argues that Ali was not afraid because the Prophet had informed them that the disbelievers would not reach him. So Ali knew he was safe.
Thus his action does not indicate greater inherent bravery.

Commentary

First, he rejects the argument based on the Prophet's statement 'The best judge among you is Ali' by analogy: the Prophet also said 'The best reciter Abu Bakr' and 'The most knowledgeable in inheritance Zaid' without that making Zaid infallible in all inheritance rulings. He argues that Abu Bakr exemplified this in his unwavering response to the Prophet's death, his firm address to the Companions, and his decisive actions against the apostates and those withholding zakah despite the odds and contrary advice. The author describes Abu Bakr's bravery as praiseworthy and approved, and dismisses the counterclaim as unsupported. Third, he addresses the argument that Ali's sleeping in the Prophet's bed shows greater courage than Abu Bakr's sadness in the cave. The author explains that Abu Bakr's sadness was for the Prophet, not fear for himself, and that Ali's fearlessness was due to prior knowledge of safety from the Prophet. While acknowledging Ali's martial prowess, the author asserts that the number of people he killed who went to Hell is insignificant compared to the multitude saved from Hellfire through Abu Bakr's guidance and leadership. Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ali's lack of fear when sleeping in the Prophet's bed proves he was braver than Abu Bakr, who was sad in the cave.
Correction: Abu Bakr's sadness was for the Prophet's safety, not his own, and Ali's fearlessness was because he knew he was protected by the Prophet's promise.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say Ali's lack of fear when sleeping in the Prophet's bed does not prove superior courage?
A) Because Ali was known to be naturally fearless
B) Because the Prophet had informed Ali that the disbelievers would not reach him
C) Because it was a less dangerous situation than the cave
D) Because Ali was asleep and unaware

Key Takeaway
To assess courage, one must consider motives and context; Abu Bakr's concern for the Prophet and Ali's divine assurance show that the incident does not indicate greater bravery for Ali.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were in Abu Bakr's place in the cave, would your fear be for yourself or for the Prophet? What does your honest answer reveal about the depth of your love for him?
?
Ali was fearless because he had certain knowledge that God would protect him. Do you live with that same certainty in God's promises? Where in your life do you let fear overcome your trust in Allah?
?
The author argues that Abu Bakr's sadness was actually a form of selfless courage. Think of a time you felt fear or sadness for someone else's sake. Did you consider that a weakness or a strength? How does this lesson change the way you view your own emotions?

Quantifying Salvation: Ali's Kills vs. Abu Bakr's Saved
Author compares the impact of Ali's kills to Abu Bakr's role in salvation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's argument that the number of people Ali killed in battle is insignificant compared to the multitude saved from Hell through Abu Bakr's efforts.

Core Idea
Abu Bakr's role in guiding people to faith outweighs Ali's military achievements.

A final argument for Ali's courage is his many kills in battle. The author does not deny this but offers a perspective: the value of saving souls from eternal punishment is far greater. This lesson examines that comparative point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on kills vs. saved
Read the final part of the Arabic and translation where the author contrasts the number of Ali's kills with those saved by Abu Bakr's guidance.
2
Understand the comparison
The author acknowledges Ali's kills but says those killed who went to Hell are less than a small portion of those saved from Hell through Abu Bakr's blessing.
This shifts the measure of virtue from martial prowess to salvific impact.
3
Reflect on the principle
The author implies that saving people from eternal punishment is a far greater virtue than killing enemies, even if the killed were harmful. This places Abu Bakr's contribution above Ali's in terms of overall benefit to humanity.

ولا تعلق لهم أيضا بقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: "أقضاكم علي" فإنه عليه السلام قال أيضا : أقرؤكم أبو بكر ، وأفرضكم زيد ولم يكن ذلك دليلا أن زيدا هو المصيب في الفرائض وغيره مبطل، فكذا هذا في القضاء، ثم كل من الصحابة كانت له فضائل جمة لا يدرك قعرها، ولكل منهم خصوصية لا يشاركه فيها غيره، ولا كلام في ذلك، وذلك لا يوجب أن من اختص بفضيلة كان أفضل من غيره من الصحابة لما في ذلك من إثبات التناقض، والله الموفق.
ودعواهم أنه أشجع الناس فنقول : إن زيادة قوة في البدن ليست فيها زيادة فضيلة، إنما الفضيلة الرباطة الجأش ، وشجاعة القلب، وترك الاكتراث بالمهالك، ولم يكن أحد في هذه المعاني مساويا لأبي بكر الصديق رضي الله عنه؛ ولهذا لم يتحير في أمره ولم يطر قلبه كل مطار عندما هجمت عليه المعضلة الصماء والخطة الدهماء بفوات نبي الرحمة، ثم خرج ثابت الجنان قوي اللسان نافذ البصيرة شديد الشكيمة فصرح بوفاة النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم، ودعاهم ( ) إلى الاعتصام بحبل الله المتين فقال: "ألا إن من كان يعبد محمدا فإن محمدا قد مات ومن كان يعبد رب محمد فإن رب محمد حي لا يموت" ثم تقلد إمامة الأمة، واستأنف الأمور بالجد والصرامة، فنفذ جيش أسامة رضي الله عنه وأرسل الجيوش إلى المرتدين ومانعي الزكاة غير مكترث بكثرة الأعداء، ولا مبال من اجتماعهم على التظاهر والتناصر والتحزب والتآلب، فقال: والله لو منعوني عقالا مما كانوا يؤدونه إلى رسول الله لقاتلتهم عليه وغيره من الصحابة رضي الله عنهم كانوا يغتنمون السلامة، ويكتفون بأن ينجو من شر الأعداء رأسا برأس، وكانوا يدعونه إلى ذلك ويشيرون له إلى ترك التعرض لهم والإعراض عنهم إلى أن تتهيأ لهم الأسباب وتنفتح عليهم إليه الأبواب، فأبت حميته في الدين، وصلابته في الإسلام وشدة توكله على ما وعد الله تعالى في كتابه، وعلى لسان رسوله الصادق المصدق من إظهار الدين ونشر الدعوة إلا التصميم على ما عزم والإصرار على ما استصوب، وهذه والله الشجاعة المحمودة والبسالة المرضية، فكان الدعوى - أن غيره أشجع منه قلبا وأربط منه جاشا وأحمى ذمارا وأقل مبالاة من المخاوف وأشد منه اقتحاما في المتالف - دعوى مرفوضة ممنوعة لا يساعدها الدليل، ولا يعاضدها البرهان.
وما تزعمون أن عليا رضي الله عنه نام على فراش النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم مع علمه بقصد الكفار ولم يخف، وأبو بكر كان يحزن في الغار . ثم نقول لهم : إن أبا بكر إنما كان يحزن لأجل رسول الله عليه السلام لا لأجل نفسه.
ألا يرى كيف فداه نفسه بإلقامه رجله الحية، ثم إن عليا لم يخف لأن النبي عليه السلام كان أخبرهم أنهم لا يصلون إليه، ولو أخبر النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم بذلك لواحد منا لا تحزن إن الله معنا لم يخف فلم يثبت بذلك زيادة شجاعة من علي.
وأما تعلقهم بكثرة من قتله علي من فرسان العرب الذين اشتهر عنادهم وكثر في الأعداء نكايتهم فلقد كان ذلك لا يجحد له جاحد غير أن من قتل علي بيديه وصار إلى النار لا يبلغ جزءا قليلا ممن استنقذهم الله تعالى ببركة أبي بكر من النيران
They have no basis either in his statement, peace and blessings be upon him: 'The best judge among you is Ali,' for he also said, 'The best reciter among you is Abu Bakr, and the most knowledgeable in inheritance among you is Zaid.' That does not prove that Zaid is correct in inheritance matters and others are wrong; so likewise this (saying) is about judgment. Then, each of the Companions had abundant virtues whose depths cannot be grasped, and each of them has a specificity that no other shares, and there is no argument about that. But that does not require that the one distinguished by a virtue is better than other Companions, because that would entail contradiction. And God is the grantor of success. And their claim that he (Ali) was the bravest of people – we say: Increase in bodily strength does not increase virtue; rather, virtue lies in steadfastness of composure, courage of the heart, and disregard for perils. And no one was equal to Abu Bakr al-Siddiq, may God be pleased with him, in these meanings. For this reason, he did not become confused in his affair nor was his heart agitated in every direction when the tremendous calamity and the awful misfortune befell him due to the passing of the Prophet of mercy. Then he emerged with firm heart, strong tongue, penetrating insight, and intense resoluteness, and he openly declared the death of the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him, and called them to hold fast to the firm rope of God, saying: 'Indeed, whoever used to worship Muhammad, Muhammad has died; and whoever used to worship the Lord of Muhammad, the Lord of Muhammad is Living and does not die.' Then he undertook the leadership of the community, and resumed affairs with seriousness and rigor. He dispatched the army of Usama, may God be pleased with him, and sent armies against the apostates and those who withheld zakah, not caring about the multitude of enemies, nor concerned about their gathering to show off, support each other, form parties, and unite. He said: 'By God, if they were to withhold from me a hobbling-cord of what they used to pay to the Messenger of God, I would fight them over it.' Other Companions, may God be pleased with them, would seize safety, and be content to escape from the evil of enemies head to head, and they invited him to that and indicated to him to leave them alone and turn away from them until circumstances become favorable and doors open for him. But his zeal for religion, his firmness in Islam, and his strong trust in what God Almighty promised in His Book and on the tongue of His truthful and believed Messenger regarding the manifestation of the religion and spread of the call, refused (anything) except determination on what he resolved and persistence on what he deemed correct. These, by God, are praiseworthy courage and approved valor. So the claim – that another is more courageous in heart, more steadfast in composure, more protective of honor, less caring of fears, and more daring in plunging into destruction – is a rejected and prohibited claim not supported by evidence, nor sustained by proof. And as for your assertion that Ali, may God be pleased with him, slept on the bed of the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him, knowing the intention of the disbelievers, and was not afraid, while Abu Bakr was sad in the cave – then we say to them: Abu Bakr was sad only for the sake of the Messenger of God, peace be upon him, not for himself. Do you not see how he sacrificed himself by placing his foot in the snake hole? Moreover, Ali was not afraid because the Prophet, peace be upon him, had informed them that they would not reach him. And if the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him, had informed anyone of us of that – 'Do not be sad, indeed God is with us' – he would not have feared. So that does not prove any increase in courage for Ali. And as for their clinging to the large number of those whom Ali killed among the knights of the Arabs who were famous for their obstinacy and whose harm among the enemies was great – indeed that is not denied by any denier. However, those whom Ali killed with his own hands and who went to the Fire do not amount to a small portion of those whom God Almighty saved from the Fire through the blessing of Abu Bakr.
Source pages p. 1197

Commentary
This passage is a sustained refutation by the author of various claims made by those who assert the superiority of Ali over Abu Bakr. The author addresses several arguments in order. First, he rejects the argument based on the Prophet's statement 'The best judge among you is Ali' by analogy: the Prophet also said 'The best reciter Abu Bakr' and 'The most knowledgeable in inheritance Zaid' without that making Zaid infallible in all inheritance rulings. So the statement about Ali does not prove his absolute superiority. Second, he refutes the claim that Ali was the bravest of people. The author distinguishes bodily strength from true courage, which is steadfastness of heart and disregard for perils. He argues that Abu Bakr exemplified this in his unwavering response to the Prophet's death, his firm address to the Companions, and his decisive actions against the apostates and those withholding zakah despite the odds and contrary advice. The author describes Abu Bakr's bravery as praiseworthy and approved, and dismisses the counterclaim as unsupported. Third, he addresses the argument that Ali's sleeping in the Prophet's bed shows greater courage than Abu Bakr's sadness in the cave. The author explains that Abu Bakr's sadness was for the Prophet, not fear for himself, and that Ali's fearlessness was due to prior knowledge of safety from the Prophet. Finally, he considers the argument about Ali's many kills in battle. While acknowledging Ali's martial prowess, the author asserts that the number of people he killed who went to Hell is insignificant compared to the multitude saved from Hellfire through Abu Bakr's guidance and leadership. Throughout, the author maintains that each Companion has unique virtues but none entail overall superiority, as that would contradict the harmony among them.

Key Terms
قتل
استنقذ
النار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the number of enemies killed in battle is the primary measure of a Companion's contribution or bravery.
Correction: The author shows that saving people from eternal damnation through guidance and leadership is a far greater virtue.

Checkpoint
How does the author compare Ali's kills to Abu Bakr's achievements?
A) He says Ali's kills were more important
B) He says both are equal in value
C) He says the number of people saved from Hell through Abu Bakr's blessing is far greater than those killed by Ali who went to Hell
D) He says Abu Bakr also killed many enemies

Key Takeaway
The ultimate measure of virtue is not how many enemies one kills but how many souls one saves from eternal punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you measure your life, do you count the 'enemies' you've defeated or the 'souls' you've helped save? Which one truly matters to you now?
?
If you were to die today, would your legacy be one of destruction (even of wrong) or one of rescue and guidance? What one step can you take this week to tip the scale toward saving?
?
This passage says Abu Bakr's blessing saved a multitude from Hell. Do you believe your own efforts could be a means of eternal rescue for others? If not, what is holding you back from seeking that role?

Refuting the Claim of Ali's Superior Bravery over Abu Bakr
Refutation of a sectarian claim about bravery

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Abu Bakr's sadness in the cave and Ali's fearlessness on the bed do not establish Ali's superior bravery, and how the author uses higher-level considerations to refute the claim.

Core Idea
True bravery is not fearlessness but adherence to divine promise.

This passage tackles a sensitive sectarian claim: that Ali's act of sleeping in the Prophet's bed shows greater bravery than Abu Bakr's conduct in the cave. The author, a Maturidi theologian, dismantles this by reinterpreting the evidence. He argues that Abu Bakr's sorrow was for the Prophet, not himself, and that Ali's calm was due to prior divine assurance. Thus, the comparison is invalid.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim being refuted
The passage starts with a general description of the Prophet's courage, then identifies the rival claim: that someone else (Ali) was braver. Identify the specific incidents mentioned: Ali on the bed, Abu Bakr in the cave.
The author rejects the claim outright as unsupported by evidence.
2
Analyze the counterargument about Abu Bakr
The author explains that Abu Bakr's sadness in the cave was for the Prophet's safety, not his own. He also mentions Abu Bakr's act of placing his foot in a snake hole to protect the Prophet.
This reframes Abu Bakr's emotion as a sign of loyalty, not cowardice.
3
Analyze the counterargument about Ali
The author points out that Ali's lack of fear was due to the Prophet's promise that the disbelievers would not harm him. If anyone had that assurance, they would not fear.
Thus, Ali's action does not prove inherent bravery higher than Abu Bakr's.
4
Synthesize the lesson
The author concludes that the claim of Ali's superior bravery is invalid because the evidence is interpreted in light of higher theological principles: divine protection and love for the Prophet.
True courage is showed by overcoming fear for the sake of Allah.

رضي الله عنهم - كانوا يغتنمون السلامة، ويكتفون بأن ينجو من شر الأعداء رأسًا برأس، وكانوا يدعونه الى ذلك ويشيرون له إلى ترك التعرض لهم والإعراض عنهم إلى أن تتهيأ لهم الأسباب وتنفتح عليهم إليه الأبواب، فأبت حميته في الدين، وصلابته في الإسلام وشدة توكله على ما وعد الله تعالى في كتابه، وعلى لسان رسوله الصادق المصدق من إظهار الدين ونشر الدعوة إلا التصميم على ما عزم والإصرار على ما استصوب، وهذه والله الشجاعة المحمودة والبسالة المرضية، فكان الدعوى - أن غيره أشجع منه قلبًا وأربط منه جاشًا وأحمى ذمارا وأقل مبالاة من المخاوف وأشد منه اقتحاماً في المتالف - دعوى مرفوضة ممنوعة لا يساعدها الدليل، ولا يعاضدها البرهان. وما تزعمون أن عليا نام على فراش النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم مع علمه بقصد الكفار ولم يخف، وأبو بكر كان يحزن في الغار . ثم نقول لهم : إن أبا بكر انه كان يحزن لأجل رسول الله عليه السلام لا لأجل نفسه. أَلا يُرى كيف فداه نفسه بإلقامه رجله الحية، ثم إن عليا لم يخف لأن النبي عليه السلام كان أخبرهم أنهم لا يصلون إليه، ولو أخبر النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم بذلك لواحد منا لا تحزن إن الله معنا لم يخف فلم يثبت بذلك زيادة شجاعة من عليه.
May Allah be pleased with them - they used to seize safety, and suffice themselves with escaping the evil of enemies head to head, and they would call him to that and indicate to him to abandon confronting them and turn away from them until the means become prepared and the doors open for him. But his zeal in religion, his firmness in Islam, and his strong trust in what Allah Almighty promised in His Book and on the tongue of His truthful, confirmed Messenger regarding the manifestation of the religion and the spread of the call, refused except determination on what he resolved and insistence on what he deemed correct. And this, by Allah, is the praiseworthy courage and the approved valor. So the claim that someone else is braver in heart, more steadfast in spirit, more protective, less concerned with fears, and more daring in perils - that claim is rejected, prohibited, not supported by evidence nor confirmed by proof. And your claim that Ali slept on the bed of the Prophet (peace be upon him) knowing the intent of the disbelievers and did not fear, while Abu Bakr was sad in the cave. Then we say to them: Indeed Abu Bakr was sad for the sake of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him), not for his own sake. Do you not see how he sacrificed himself by putting his foot in the snake hole? Then indeed Ali did not fear because the Prophet (peace be upon him) had informed them that they would not reach him. And if the Prophet (peace be upon him) had informed that to any of us, 'Do not grieve, indeed Allah is with us,' he would not fear. So by that, an increase in bravery is not established from him.
Source pages p. 1198

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a claim that Ali was braver than Abu Bakr. The author begins by describing the Prophet's own steadfast courage and rebuffs any notion that anyone could surpass him. He then addresses the specific argument: Ali slept on the Prophet's bed fearlessly while Abu Bakr was sad in the cave. The author provides two counterpoints: first, Abu Bakr's sadness was out of concern for the Prophet, not for himself, and he demonstrated courage by protecting the Prophet from a snake. Second, Ali's lack of fear was due to the Prophet's assurance that the disbelievers would not reach him. Thus, the incident does not prove Ali's superior bravery over Abu Bakr.

Misconception: Abu Bakr's sadness in the cave indicates cowardice.
Correction: The author clarifies that Abu Bakr's sadness was out of concern for the Prophet, not for himself, and he demonstrated bravery by protecting the Prophet from a snake.

Checkpoint
Why was Abu Bakr sad in the cave according to the author?
A) He feared for his own life.
B) He feared for the Prophet's safety.
C) He was worried about the snake.
D) He was sad because Ali was not there.

Key Takeaway
The claim of Ali's superior bravery over Abu Bakr is invalid because the evidence is misinterpreted: Abu Bakr's sadness was for the Prophet, and Ali's fearlessness was due to divine assurance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Think of a time you judged someone's faith by their visible calmness. How might their inner struggle for the sake of others, like Abu Bakr's sadness for the Prophet, be a greater act of bravery than apparent fearlessness?

The Superiority of Guiding People Over Killing Enemies
The author argues that Abu Bakr's guidance of countless people to Islam outweighs Ali's killing of enemies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that guiding people to Islam is superior to military victories, as illustrated by the comparison between Abu Bakr and Ali.

Core Idea
Guiding one person to faith is better than killing countless enemies.

This passage presents a theological argument from the Maturidi tradition that counters claims of superiority based on military achievements. The author uses the example of Abu Bakr's success in bringing many to Islam versus Ali's kills, supporting it with a hadith. This lesson will unpack the reasoning and its implications for understanding virtue in Islam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Get a sense of the flow and main claim.
The passage is a single continuous argument.
2
Identify the two sides compared
The author compares Abu Bakr's guidance of multitudes to Ali's killing of Arab knights. Note how the author frames the comparison.
Abu Bakr's guidance is linked to 'blessing' (baraka).
3
Understand the reasoning against killing
The author explains that killing only breaks power and repels harm, whereas guidance brings the added benefits of increasing the ummah and fulfilling the Prophet's boast.
The hadith about 'boasting before nations' is cited.
4
Reflect on the key hadith
The Prophet told Ali that guiding one person is better than killing vast numbers. See how the author uses this to elevate guidance over killing.
This hadith is central to the argument.
5
Consider the prophetic mission
The author concludes by noting that prophets were sent to call people to faith, not to kill. Therefore, the one who brings people to Islam without war has a greater rank.
This reinforces the superiority of guidance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما تعلقهم بكثرة من قتله على من فرسان العرب الذين اشتهر عنادهم وكثر في الأعداء نكايتهم فلقد كان ذلك لا يجحد له جاحد غير أن من قتل علي بيديه وصار إلى النار لا يبلغ جزءًا قليلاً ممن استنقذهم الله تعالى ببركة أبي بكر من النيران وأدخله في زمرة المسلمين، فإن من سبق ذكره من كبار الصحابة آمنوا كلهم ببركة دعوته، ومن سواهم ممن لا يحصون، وكذا أكثر العرب ارتدوا ، ثم إن الله تعالى هداهم بيمن نقيبته وبركة إمامته، ولا شك أن هذا أفضل من القتل إذ ليس في قتل الكفار إلا كسر شوكتهم ودفع معرتهم، وفي هدايتهم يحصل هذا ثم تكثر به الأمة فيكون سببًا لتحقيق مباهاة الرسول عليه السلام بقوله: 'فإني أباهي بكم الأمم يوم القيامة' وتشد به قوة أهل الإسلام ويلتف جميعهم ويكثر حزب الله المفلحون، ولهذا قال رسول الله عليه السلام لعلي: 'لو هدي على يديك أحد لكان خيرا لك من أن تقتل ما بين المشرق والمغرب' ثم كيف لا يكون كدلك وقد بعثت الرسل عليهم السلام للدعوة إلى الدين، وله جعلت المحاربات وأنواع الجهاد، فمن يسلم على يديه أحد من غير كلفه حرب ولا عناء وجهاد كان ذلك أعظم محل له وأجل قدر في الإسلام، ثم إذا كان قتل ما بين المشرق والمغرب لا يوازي هداية واحد بشهادة الرسول عليه السلام، فكيف يوازي قتل نفر معدودين من لا يدخل تحت الحصر والعد ، ولهذا جلت مرتبة الرسل عليهم السلام وإن لم يباشروا قتل أحد من الأعداء أو باشروا قتل قليل منهم.
As for their attachment to the many whom Ali killed from the Arab knights who were famous for their stubbornness and great infliction among the enemies, indeed that cannot be denied, except that those whom Ali killed with his own hands and who went to the Fire do not amount to a small fraction of those whom Allah Almighty saved through the blessing of Abu Bakr from the Fires and brought into the ranks of the Muslims. For those mentioned earlier from the major companions all believed through the blessing of his call, and others beyond counting, and likewise most of the Arabs apostatized, then Allah Almighty guided them through the blessing of his leadership and the blessing of his imamate. And there is no doubt that this is better than killing, for in killing disbelievers there is only breaking their power and repelling their harm, while in their guidance this is achieved and then the nation increases by them, so it becomes a cause for the fulfillment of the Prophet's (peace be upon him) boasting: 'Indeed I boast of you before the nations on the Day of Resurrection.' And the strength of the people of Islam is thereby reinforced, they all gather, and the successful party of Allah multiplies. For this reason, the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said to Ali: 'If a person were guided at your hands, that would be better for you than killing what is between the east and the west.' Then how could it not be so, while the Messengers (peace be upon them) were sent for the call to religion, and for that warfare and types of jihad were prescribed. So whoever causes a person to become Muslim without the trouble of war and hardship and jihad, that is a greater station for him and a higher status in Islam. Then if killing what is between the east and the west does not equal the guidance of one person according to the testimony of the Prophet (peace be upon him), then how can it equal the killing of a few individuals who cannot be counted? For this reason, the rank of the Messengers (peace be upon them) is elevated even if they did not personally kill any enemy or killed few of them.
Source pages p. 1199

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that Ali's military achievements make him superior to Abu Bakr. The author argues that the number of people guided to Islam through Abu Bakr's efforts is far greater than the number of enemies Ali killed. He cites the hadith that guiding one person is better than killing vast numbers. The argument proceeds: killing only breaks power and repels harm, while guidance increases the ummah, fulfills the Prophet's pride, and strengthens Islam. The prophets' mission was to call people to faith, not to kill, so the one who brings people to Islam without war is greater. Thus, Abu Bakr's guidance of countless souls far surpasses Ali's kills.

Key Terms
هداية
قتل
بركة
جهاد
مباهاة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Military prowess is the highest virtue in Islam.
Correction: The author argues that guiding people to faith brings greater benefit to the ummah and fulfills the Prophet's pride, making it superior to military achievements.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the main purpose of sending prophets?
A) To kill enemies
B) To call to religion
C) To establish kingdoms
D) To perform miracles

Key Takeaway
The Islamic virtue of guiding people to faith is considered greater than military achievements because it increases the community, fulfills the Prophet's mission, and is the very purpose for which prophets were sent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you had to choose between an act of 'worship' that involves physical struggle and an act that brings one person to Islam, which would you invest your time in? How does this passage challenge your assumptions about what truly counts in Allah's scale?
?
Think of the people you interact with daily. How many are you actively guiding towards faith? If the Prophet ﷺ said that guiding one person is better than killing all between east and west, what does that say about how you spend your time and energy? Write one concrete step you will take this week to guide someone.
?
This passage elevates Abu Bakr's role as a guide over Ali's role as a warrior. Are you building a legacy of guiding or of 'fighting' in your life? What change must you make today to align with the higher virtue?

Defending the Ranking of Uthman
Author defends Maturidi ranking of Uthman after Abu Bakr and Umar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi position on the ranking of Uthman and the evidence used to defend it.

Core Idea
The Maturidis defend Uthman's superiority after Abu Bakr and Umar using merits and explicit hadith.

This passage presents the Maturidi defense of Uthman's rank as third after Abu Bakr and Umar. The author states the school's position, mentions dissenting views, lists Uthman's merits, and cites hadith that explicitly state the order. The lesson will guide you through the argument and evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the overall argument.
Notice the structure: position, dissenting views, merits, refutations, hadith.
2
Identify Uthman's merits
List the specific merits mentioned: spending, Pledge of Ridwan, angels' shyness, behavior at Uhud, courage, Day of Daar, conquests.
The author uses these to counter objections.
3
Examine the hadith evidence
Note the three hadith from Abu Dawud that directly state the order of virtue.
These hadith are the primary textual proof.
4
Reflect on the argument
Consider how the author addresses potential doubts (e.g., Uhud) and establishes the ranking.
The conclusion ties all evidence together.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في فضل عثمان: ظاهر مذهب أصحابنا القول بتفضيل عثمان رضي الله عنه بعد أبي بكر وعمر، وذهب الحسين بن الفضل البجلي ومحمد بن إسحاق بن خزيمة من أهل الحديث إلى تفضيل علي على عثمان، وتوقف أبو العباس القلانسي في ذلك وكان يرى إمامة المفضول جائزة، ومناقبه كثيرة، وبذله الأموال في نصرة دين الله وإقامة النبي عليه السلام إحدى يديه مقام يد عثمان في بيعة الرضوان معروفة، والأخبار بأن الملائكة تستحي منه مشهورة، ثم انحيازه في حرب أحد لا يدل على جبنه إذ قد يتفق ذلك أحيانا للبطل الكمي لعارض أمر خفي، ثم الشجاعة ليست إلا التهاون بالموت وعدم المبالاة من التلف والهلاك، وقد ظهر ذلك منه يوم الدار من منعة ناصريه عن القتال، وتعليق عتق عبيده بإلقاء السلاح على وجه لا تسمح به نفس أشهر خليقة الله بالشجاعة. ثم ما ظهر من الفتوح في أيامه من قبل المغرب على يدي عماله وأصحاب جيوشه، وذلك خير له من قتل ألوف من أبناء الحرب فيما بين الطعن والضرب، والذي يؤيد ما ذهبنا إليه ما روى أبو داود السجستاني في كتاب السنن بإسناده رضي الله عنهما عن ابن عمر أنه قال: - كنا نقول في زمن النبي عليه السلام: لا يعدل بأبي بكر أحد ثم عمر ثم عثمان. وروى أيضا عن ابن عمر أنه قال: - كنا نقول ورسول الله عليه السلام حي: أفضل أمة النبي عليه السلام بعده أبو بكر ثم عمر ثم عثمان. وروى أبو داود أيضا عن محمد بن الحنفية: قال: قلت يا أبي أي الناس خير بعد رسول الله ؟ قال : أبو بكر، قلت: ثم من؟ قال : عمر، قال: ثم خشيت أن أقول: ثم من، فيقول : عثمان، فقلت أنت يا أبت؟ قال ما أنا إلا رجل من المسلمين. فثبت بهذه الأحاديث ما ادعينا من ترتيب الفضيلة، والله الموفق.
Discussion on the preference of Uthman: The apparent doctrine of our companions is the statement of preferring Uthman, may Allah be pleased with him, after Abu Bakr and Umar. And al-Husayn ibn al-Fadl al-Bajali and Muhammad ibn Ishaq ibn Khuzayma from the people of hadith went to preferring Ali over Uthman. And Abu al-Abbas al-Qalanisi hesitated in that and used to consider the imamate of the inferior one permissible. His merits are many, and his spending of wealth in supporting the religion of Allah and the Prophet, peace be upon him, placing one of his hands in place of Uthman's hand in the Pledge of Ridwan is well-known, and the reports that the angels feel shy from him are famous. Then his staying back in the Battle of Uhud does not indicate cowardice, for that may sometimes occur to the fearless hero due to a hidden cause. Then courage is nothing but disregard for death and lack of concern for destruction and ruin. And that appeared from him on the Day of ad-Dar from his preventing his supporters from fighting, and his conditioning the freeing of his slaves upon laying down arms in a manner that the soul of the most famous of Allah's creation for courage would not permit. Then what appeared of conquests in his days from the direction of the Maghrib at the hands of his governors and companions of his armies, and that is better for him than killing thousands of children of war amidst thrusting and striking. And what supports what we have gone to is what Abu Dawud al-Sijistani narrated in the Book of Sunan with his chain, may Allah be pleased with them, from Ibn Umar that he said: We used to say in the time of the Prophet, peace be upon him: No one is equal to Abu Bakr, then Umar, then Uthman. And he also narrated from Ibn Umar that he said: We used to say while the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, was alive: The best of the nation of the Prophet after him is Abu Bakr, then Umar, then Uthman. And Abu Dawud also narrated from Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyya: He said: I said, O my father, who is the best of people after the Messenger of Allah? He said: Abu Bakr. I said: Then who? He said: Umar. He said: Then I feared to say: Then who, and he would say: Uthman, so I said: Are you, O my father? He said: I am only a man from the Muslims. So by these hadiths is established what we claimed of the order of virtue. And Allah is the Granter of success.
Source pages p. 1201

Commentary
This passage is a theological defense of the ranking of Uthman ibn Affan as the third most virtuous Companion after Abu Bakr and Umar. The author first states the position of his school (the Maturidis) that Uthman is superior to Ali. He then mentions dissenting views: some hadith scholars (al-Bajali and Ibn Khuzayma) preferred Ali over Uthman, and al-Qalanisi suspended judgment. The author then lists Uthman's merits: his generous spending for Islam, the Prophet's substitution of his hand for Uthman's in the Pledge of Ridwan, reports that angels feel shy from him, his behavior at Uhud (which is explained as not cowardice but a rare occurrence for even a brave man), his courage demonstrated on the Day of the House (when he prevented fighting and offered to free slaves if arms were laid down), and the conquests during his caliphate. Finally, he cites three hadith from Abu Dawud that explicitly state the order of virtue: Abu Bakr, then Umar, then Uthman. The passage concludes that these hadith establish the claimed ranking.

Key Terms
تفضيل
بيعة الرضوان
شجاعة
ترتيب الفضيلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Maturidi school prefers Ali over Uthman.
Correction: This passage shows the Maturidi position is that Uthman is superior to Ali after Abu Bakr and Umar.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is a hadith cited by the author to support the order of virtue?
A) 'The best of this ummah after its Prophet is Abu Bakr, then Umar, then Uthman.'
B) 'Ali is the best of the Companions.'
C) 'Uthman is better than Abu Bakr.'
D) 'The angels feel shy from Abu Bakr.'

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi position ranks Uthman third in virtue after Abu Bakr and Umar, supported by explicit hadith and Uthman's notable merits.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you find yourself inclined to take sides in debates about the Companions based on your personal preferences or the group you follow, rather than on explicit texts? Will you today commit to examining your heart and setting aside any partisan loyalty that contradicts authentic hadith?
?
Uthman’s courage was shown through restraint and self-sacrifice. In your own life, where are you tempted to prove your strength through aggression or harshness, rather than through patience and self-control? Will you take one concrete step this week to imitate Uthman’s forbearance?
?
The author uses hadith as decisive evidence. When you encounter a clear text from the sunnah that challenges a deeply held opinion, do you resist it? What is one opinion you have held that you must now surrender to the authority of the Prophet’s words?

Clarifying the Scope on Ali's Preference
The author clarifies his stance on Ali's preference and the limited scope of his book.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's affirmation of Ali's unique merits and his methodological clarification that the book's purpose is to rank the four caliphs, not to exhaustively list all Companion virtues.

Core Idea
Ali's superiority is affirmed, but the book's purpose is narrower: ordering the caliphs' virtue.

In this passage, the author addresses a potential misunderstanding. He strongly affirms Ali's unique merits among his contemporaries, but then clarifies that his book was not intended to catalogue all Companion virtues. Instead, its goal was to establish the ranking of the four Rightly Guided Caliphs, a task already completed. This prevents readers from expecting extensive biographical praise.

Guided Path
1
Notice the author's strong affirmation of Ali's superiority
Read how the author states that no rational person would deny Ali's preference over all others of his time, due to his unique combination of qualities.
The author uses a hyperbolic expression 'لا أعلم أحدًا' to emphasize the obviousness of this.
2
Identify the author's clarification of his book's purpose
Read the sentence where the author says the purpose of his book was not to explain virtues of Companions, but to explain the order of virtue among the caliphs.
This is a key methodological point: the book has a limited scope.
3
Reflect on the author's closing directive
The author directs readers to earlier books for detailed virtues, implying his work is not exhaustive.
His emphasis is on the ranking, not comprehensive praise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في تفضيل علي: لا أعلم أحدًا يرجع إلى عقل وعلم يمتنع من تفضيل علي رضي الله عنه على جميع أهل زمان خلافته إذ لم يجتمع من أحد منهم ما اجتمع فيه من العلم والورع والاجتهاد في الدين والشجاعة، وليس الغرض من كتابنا هذا بيان فضائل الصحابة لنشتغل بذلك بل كان غرضنا بيان الترتيب في الفضيلة بين الخلفاء الراشدين. وقد فرغنا من ذلك بحمد الله تعالى - فلا معنى للإطالة ببيان فضيلة كل واحد منهم إذ كتب السلف مشحونة بذلك، فمن رام الوقوف عليه فلينظر فيها، والله الهادي للعباد إلى سبيل الرشاد.
Discussion on the preference of Ali: I do not know anyone who returns to intellect and knowledge who would refrain from preferring Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, over all the people of his time of caliphate, for there did not combine in any one of them what combined in him of knowledge, piety, diligence in religion, and courage. And the purpose of our book is not the exposition of the merits of the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, that we should occupy ourselves with that, but rather our purpose was to explain the order of virtue among the Rightly Guided Caliphs. And we have finished that by the praise of Allah, the Exalted - so there is no meaning in prolonging by explaining the virtue of each one of them, for the books of the predecessors are filled with that. So whoever desires to come upon it, let him look into them. And Allah is the Guide of the servants to the path of right guidance.
Source pages p. 1202

Commentary
This passage is a brief methodological clarification. The author asserts that no rational and learned person would deny Ali's superiority over all others of his caliphal era, because of his unique combination of knowledge, piety, diligence, and courage. However, he immediately states that the purpose of his book was not to detail the virtues of all Companions, but only to establish the order of virtue among the four Rightly Guided Caliphs. Since he has already completed that objective (namely, the ranking of Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, and Ali), there is no need to elaborate further on each one's merits. He directs the reader to the abundant earlier works for such details.

Key Terms
تفضيل
الخلفاء الراشدين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author intended to deny Ali's superiority because he limits his discussion of virtues.
Correction: The author explicitly affirms Ali's unique superiority over his contemporaries, but explains that his book's scope is limited to ranking the caliphs, not detailing all virtues.

Checkpoint
What does the author say is the purpose of his book regarding the Companions?
A) To detail the virtues of all Companions.
B) To prove Ali is the best.
C) To explain the order of virtue among the four caliphs.
D) To defend the position of the Kharijites.

Key Takeaway
Ali's unique merits are affirmed, but the author's work is specifically about the ranking of the four caliphs, not a comprehensive virtue catalog.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you admire someone's virtues—like the author admires Ali's knowledge, piety, diligence, and courage—do you let that admiration distract you from the specific task Allah has placed before you? Or do you, like the author, recognize excellence while staying focused on your own limited responsibility?
?
Are you quick to get lost in every side discussion and virtue story when studying your faith, or do you discipline yourself to grasp the specific lesson Allah intends from each text? What would change if you treated every verse and hadith as having a precise purpose, not as material for unbounded admiration?
?
The author points to the books of the predecessors, saying 'they are filled with that.' Do you humbly refer to those who came before for deeper knowledge, or do you feel the need to have everything spelled out for you? What does your attitude reveal about your reliance on Allah's guidance through the scholars of the past?
